Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Live-stock Slaughter Levy Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 82 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-stock Slaughter Levy Act 1964.
Loan ACt(No. 2) 1982 (Act No. 107 of 1982)An Act to authorize the borrowing and expending of moneys for defence purposes.
National Crimes Commission Act 1982 (Act No. 138 of 1982)An Act to establish a National Crimes Commission to investigate criminal activities, in particular organized criminal activities, with a view to the prosecution of offenders.
Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.
Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 128 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Act 1971.
Public Service Acts Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. III of 1982)- An Act relating to the Australian Public Service.
Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 840f 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No. I) 1930.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 85 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No.2) 1930.

Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
POLAND: DEMOCRATIC RIGHTS
Notice of Motion
Mr SCHOLES (Corio)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I will move:
That members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament-
(I) convey to the Governments of Poland and the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics-
(a) the members' serious concern at the continued detention of trade union leader Lech Walesa, and
(b) the belief that this detention(i) constitutes a denial of basic human rights, and (ii) contravenes the right of workers to organise themselves into trade unions and associations to speak and act on their behalf, and
(2) call for the immediate release of Mr Walesa and the restoration of the Polish people's democratic rights and freedoms asset out in the United Nations Charter.
INCOME TAX LAWS
Notice of Motion
Mr GROOM (Braddon)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House-
(I) calls for all laws to be simple to understand and administer;
(2) expresses its concern-
(a) that while the 1936 Income Tax Assessment Act comprised only 85 pages and the 1973 reprint of the Act 555 pages, the present Act consists of 1,092 pages, and
(b) that since 1975 there have been 69 amendments to the Act comprising 1,047 substantive pages of new tax law,and
(3) further calls for the establishment of a task force of practical people to overhaul completely the income tax laws with a view to making them equitable, effective and simple.
POLAND: DEMOCRATIC RIGHTS
Notice of Motion
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House-
( I) notes with concern recent reports of suppression by Polish Martial Law Authorities of peaceful demonstrations in Poland which sought the restoration of political rights and the release of political prisoners, and
(2) calls upon the Polish Martial Law Authorities to-
(a) end martial law in Poland; (b) free Lech Walesa and other detainees associated with
the Solidarity Free Trade Union, and, (c) recognise the right of free and democratic unions to
operate under the terms of the Gdansk agreement.

DISALLOWED QUESTION
Mr Armitage proceeding to address a question to the Prime Minister--
Mr SPEAKER-The question is out of order.
Mr Hayden-Mr Speaker, I take a point of order. I ask why the question is out of order.
Mr SPEAKER-The question is out of order because I will not allow questions to be asked on matters for which the person to whom the question is addressed is not responsible to this House. I think it is very important that that principle be observed, otherwise issues between the parliamentary party and the organisational party will be a matter for ventilation in this House when there is no responsibility for them on the part of a Minister in the House.
Mr Hayden-Mr Speaker, in that case, I take a further point of order. I am sure that there was some murmuring and perhaps you did not hear the question clearly. The thrust of the honourable member's question very simply was to refer to statements that the Prime Minister made in this House yesterday when he quoted from a document. The honourable member has proceeded no further than to indicate that the Prime Minister quoted from a document to which he resorted. In those circumstances it would be rather odd if the question could not proceed.
Mr SPEAKER-It would not be odd. The fact is--
Mr Hayden-A normal, reasonable--
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman is a very reasonable person. Therefore, he will listen to me while I make the explanation. The fact is that the Standing Orders put certain boundaries upon questions. They do not put the same boundaries upon what an honourable member is entitled to say in the course of a debate so it does not make a question in order to quote from what an honourable member said in a debate. The question is out of order.
Mr Armitage-Mr Speaker, I take a point of order. This question is most important. It tests the veracity of statements by the Prime Minister in the Parliament yesterday. It is obvious from the Press this morning that he was not telling the truth.
Mr SPEAKER-The importance of the question is not the issue.

Questions without Notice

AGRICULTURAL TRACTORS
Mr TUCKEY-I address my question to the Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce. I refer him to correspondence that he and his Department have received from me over the past 18 months relating to the Phillips tractor company of Merredin in agricultural Western Australia. It is a small but viable manufacturer of four wheel drive agricultural tractors ranging in value from $70,000 to $250,000. Is it a fact that this company must pay the 2 per cent special revenue duty on all its imported mechanical components, thus increasing costs by $600 to $900 per tractor? Is it a fact that all imported four wheel drive tractors are free of all duty, including the revenue duty, and therefore have a substantial competitive advantage over the Australian product? Further, can arrangements similar to those announced for the shipping industry be made for this viable and efficient manufacturer, thus alleviating this obvious injustice?
Mr HODGMAN-Whilst the honourable member was kind enough to tell me that he would ask this question, it is not a Dorothy Dixer because he is critical of an aspect of Government policy which I feel ought to be debated by the House and certainly taken into account. The 2 per cent revenue duty which was imposed by the Government on 24 May 1979 does apply. That is the response to the first part of the honourable member's question. In respect of the second part of his question the answer is also yes. It is fair to say that the question of this duty was considered this year by the Government in the Budget context, but because of monetary constraints it was not possible to remove the duty. Lastly, I say to the honourable member in relation to the specifics of the case that, if the company concerned is able to raise the Australian content of its product to 55 per cent, which is the minimum required, the company will be eligible for the current bounty assistance. Unfortunately, at present the company's Australian content is only 43 per cent. Unless it can bridge the gap between 43 per cent and 55 per cent the benefit will not be available.
TAX MINIMISATION
Mr WALLIS-Is the Prime Minister aware of a tax minimisation scheme which is both very sophisticated and very lucrative for the South Australian branch of the Liberal Party? Has the Prime Minister been consulted by the Minister for Veterans' Affairs about the role played by the Minister in setting up this scheme? Has the Minister told the Prime Minister whether the scheme can be classed as tax evasion or only tax avoidance? Has the Minister, as Minister Assisting the

Treasurer, advised the Prime Minister what the cost of this scheme will be to the taxpayers?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I have had no advice of any kind related to the information that was in the honourable member's question. One can only assume that the honourable member, in asking a question of that kind, is continuing the totally false smear tactics of the Leader of the Opposition.
NORTHERN TERRITORY POSTCODES
Mr TAMBLING-I ask the Minister for Communications: What stage has Australia Post reached in its review of postcodes which I was advised was under way some six months ago? Can the Minister advise whether Australia Post has reached a decision on the allocation of a more distinctive range of postcodes for the Northern Territory? When will the Northern Territory be divorced from the 5000 range which is applicable in South Australia? What particular recognition will be given to all Darwin suburbs?
Mr N. A. BROWN-I am not personally aware of all the precise postcodes in the Northern Territory, but I am doing my best to memorise them lest I be made the subject of an attack by the Opposition. The specific matter raised by the honourable member has been under investigation and review by the Australian Postal Commission, and that review is considerably advanced. I have received several reports from the Postal Commission. Amongst other things, the Commission is taking into account, as I am, the very strong representations made by the honourable member. I know that the honourable member feels that, with the emergence of the Northern Territory as a very significant geographical and political entity in its own right, the people of the Northern Territory, particularly Darwin, should have distinctive postcodes. I share that view and I am very grateful that the honourable member should have pursued the matter with so much zeal and resolution. I can assure him that the case that he has advanced will certainly be examined.
ALLEGATIONS AGAINST MINISTER
Dr EVERINGHAM-I refer the Minister for Administrative Services to his obscure statement of 31 August last in which he announced the exoneration of an unidentified Minister from unspecified allegations. Were these allegations serious enough to warrant investigation by Federal and Victorian police and other government instrumentalities? Did the Minister order this investigation on his own initiative or after consultation with or at the behest of another Minister? Did the Minister decide on his own initiative that

TAX EVASION
Mr ARMITAGE-My question is directed to the Prime Minister. I refer to the Prime Minister's statement during the debate on yesterday's censure motion in which he claimed that he had obtained unanimous support for the Government's proposed retrospective legislation against bottom-of-the-harbour tax evasion and indeed quoted documents to support this claim. Has the

Prime Minister seen a statement that one of those who he claimed supported the Government's legislation has since dissociated himself from the Government's proposed action? In the light of this contradiction is the Prime Minister prepared to restate his claim or to revise it?
An Opposition member-Or tell the truth.
Mr SPEAKER-I did not notice who made that interjection but it was unparliamentary.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I made it perfectly plain that all State presidents of the Liberal Party supported the statement put out in the name of the Federal Executive, and that remains so. As a result of some misinterpretation of that statement in the media the Federal President put out a further letter which was obviously his own statement. In my view that letter is identical in its content and sense to the statement. I understand from what I read in one newspaper this morning that a certain State president may have dissociated himself from that letter. If he has I can only suggest that he wait and contain himself until the colour of the legislation is seen.
EXPORTS: AUSTRALIA'S INTERNATIONAL REPUTATION
Mr SPENDER-Has the attention of the Deputy Prime Minister been drawn to an article in the Australian Financial Review of 7 September concerning a resources development and supply conference in Sydney and to what was reported to have been said there by Mr Imai of Nippon Steel Corporation who, amongst other things, was critical, so it appears, of Australia's high costs and growing reputation for unreliability as an exporter? Can the Deputy Prime Minister tell the House the implications for the future prosperity of this country of increases in costs in our export industries, particularly iron and coal, and the increase in our growing reputation for unreliability as an exporter?
Mr ANTHONY-I have not seen the actual report but it has been brought to my attention that concern was expressed by some of the Australians at the conference about the possibility of declining imports by Japan of our iron ore and coal. I think one has to recognise the realities of the situation in that the Japanese steel industry looks like going through its lowest production year for the last 11 years. This is because of both lower consumption in Japan and lower export possibilities. That is affecting our sales of iron ore from Western Australia and coking coal from the eastern States. What Australia has been advocating in making representations to Japan is that

1158 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Questions without Notice

there should be a sharing of any decline in the imports ofraw materials. We have developed a very substantial iron and coal exporting industry in parallel with the growth of the Japanese steel industry. If there are any reversals we would want to see the same equitable proportion going to Japan as we have traditionally had.
At the Australia-Japan Ministerial Committee meeting that we had recently I made the point very cogently that we did not want to see our percentage share of the market being lost. Traditionally we have had about 48 per cent of the iron ore market. It is a bit lower than that this year and we would like to see that picked up. Traditionally, we have been getting from 50 per cent to 55 per cent of the coking coal market. So concerned is Western Australia about the matter that its Minister for Resources Development was in Japan only last week pointing out that if Western Australia were to get any lesser share of the market it would seriously jeopardise the economic operations of its iron ore exports.
The Premier of New South Wales is also very concerned about the matter. At the moment he is in Japan and will be making similar representations. I think the greatest damage that has been done to our reputation over the last year was caused by the surge of industrial disputes and problems. They led to shipping delays. Fortunately in New South Wales there has been some reprieve from that situation. The long waiting time, resulting in heavy demurrage paid on ships off Newcastle and Port Kembla, has been eased. There has been a responsible response from the unions as a result of discussions between governments and the industry. I am hoping that the unions will recognise how important it is that from now on they maintain the orders that they have; that they try to reinstate the reliability of Australia as a dependable exporter. I cannot emphasise that enough. At present, when there are difficult export circumstances around the world, Australia has to struggle to its utmost to maintain its competitive position, and reliability plays an important part.
TAX DODGERS
Mr JOHN BROWN-Did the Prime Minister say in a speech in Adelaide last Sunday week that all tax dodgers had to get out of the Liberal Party? Have any of them left yet? When, if ever, is the Prime Minister going to direct the Treasurer and other Ministers to dismiss one notorious tax dodger from the seven discretionary and lucrative public offices that this Government has bestowed upon him?

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-What I have said on this issue has been perfectly plain. I have indicated on more than one occasion that the Government is going to pursue those who have profited from bottom-of-the-harbour schemes with all the force of the law that we can command. A special prosecutor is being established and that legislation will be introduced into the Parliament. I have also indicated that if people who have benefited from those kinds of schemes are within the Liberal Party, I would prefer them to be outside it.
VIETNAMESE IMMIGRATION
Mr JARMAN-Can the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs advise what progress has been made with the Vietnamese authorities concerning the processing of people for migrant entry from that country?
Mr HODGES-At the outset I should point out to the House that the orderly departure program of migrants from Vietnam is distinctly different from the refugee program that we have been pursuing for a number of years. I am pleased to report to the House that officers of my Department have commenced the processing of some 450 people in Ho Chi Minh City for migrant entry out of a total of approximately 6,000 people who have been sponsored in Australia. I point out to the House also that the cost of passage of these people will have to be borne by the migrants or by their sponsors in Australia. This is distinct from the refugee program, under which the costs of the passage are borne by the Government. If current progress is maintained I expect the first arrivals within the next two to three months. These people come under the Family Migration Program and are being sponsored by Australians in exactly the same way as are people coming from any other part of the world. It is interesting to note that the present position has been reached after four years of negotiations with the Vietnamese Government.
ORGANISED CRIME
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I refer the Minister for Administrative Services to the Minister's participation in a television program on Channel 9 on 9 May last, within a very short time of this House completing its autumn sittings. I ask the Minister whether he said in the course of that interview that he had evidence that State politicians and businessmen were involved in organised crime and, further, that organised crime was involved in drug trafficking, terrorism, international money dealing, arms trafficking and corruption of police and public officials? Did he further say in the

Questions without Notice

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1159

course of the interview that he could have used at least five sitting days in Parliament to have a real fling at a couple of people or governments? In view of the fact that Parliament is back in session and has been for three weeks I now ask: When does the Minister propose to make a statement indicating the nature of the evidence that he has, the identity of the politicians to whom he referred and the details of drug trafficking, international money dealing, arms trafficking and corruption of police and public officials?
Mr NEWMAN-The Deputy Leader of the Opposition is really tempting me. If he needs any evidence or any description of the things which he just detailed I think he should go to chapter 10 of the report of the Costigan Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union which we released last night and he will see exactly what I am getting at. But if the honourable member wants me to have a fling at a couple of people I will tell him what I had in mind. In this place over the last couple of weeks we have had allegations by the Leader of the Opposition and the people behind him that somehow there has been a dereliction of duty by Ministers on this side of the House, that they should have known certain things that were going on in departments. I ask the Deputy Leader of the Opposition to consider this point: The Premier of New South Wales, Mr Wran, should be well questioned about his position in the expected appointment of a Commissioner of Police which fortunately did not happen. Mr Wran should be asked why that was going to happen. Did he know about the activity of the man in question? Did he sanction it? Did he choose that man? If they are the sorts of things the honourable member wants me to have a fling at, there it is. I think they are reasonable questions to ask the Government of New South Wales. They are certainly reasonable questions to ask Mr Wran.
GRIFFITH UNIVERSITY
Mr ADERMANN-I preface my question to the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security by pointing out that unemployment benefits are not available to students of tertiary institutions during recesses. Is the Minister aware that the buildings and the campus at Griffith University in Queensland have been required and appropriated for the Commonwealth Games activities? Is it a fact, therefore, that the academic year ends at Griffith University during this month of September, over two months earlier than normal, and the university will not be reopening until the end of February next year? Is this an inordinately longer recess than was comprehended by the

guidelines? Will the Minister, perhaps iIi conjunction with the Minister for Education, consider the special circumstances of continuing students at Griffith University who complete their academic year in September and do not commence studies again until February and who are for that whole time ineligible for unemployment benefits?
Mr WILSON-I state in response to the honourable member's question that I will make inquiries into the implications of that decision. I think that the circumstances for those students who may have a longer summer vacation because of the Commonwealth Games are really no different from those of students attending universities where the year is in accordance with the normal pattern and not interrupted by the Commonwealth Games. I will, however, look at the implications of the matter by referring it to my colleague in another place. I think that their circumstances are no different from those of other students. In fact, they would have an opportunity, as many students do, to seek vacation work of one sort or another to supplement the income they may be receiving from other sources.
AUDITOR-GENERAVS REPORT ON DAIRY REBATES
Mr HAYDEN-I ask the Prime Minister a question. I refer to a statement to this House almost a year ago, on 29 October 1981 to be precise, by the Minister for Primary Industry in which that Minister said that he was directing the Auditor-General to carry out a full investigation of the scandal on rebates paid by the Australian Dairy Corporation on skim milk sales to an overseas corporation. Has the Prime Minister noted that the Auditor-General in his annual report tabled yesterday discloses that he completed this investigation and sent the report to the Minister for Primary Industry almost five months ago, on 27 April this year? Why has the Government not tabled this report in Parliament? Is it because the Government is gravely embarrassed by its findings, particularly as they concern the Minister for Primary Industry's administration of his Department?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Minister is opening a Cattlemen's Union conference in Mackay today. He will be here tomorrow. I suggest that the Leader of the Opposition address his question to him then.

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-As a result of the debate in Parliament yesterday and also as a result of things that were said outside it, there was a clear assumption by many people, a clear assumption by the media-by the Courier-Mail, the Australian and a number of others-that three knights had been named in the unpublished documents of the Costigan Royal Commission. That was the clear assumption that they made. One has only to read the reports to know that that was so. lf one does read what the Leader of the Opposition said in the Parliament yesterday, it would seem to be very clear that he intended to give that impression. Whether his words are open to more than one interpretation is another matter, but it would seem very plain that he intended to give that impression and that impression was drawn by the media in very plain and stark terms.
The Leader of the Opposition at one point yesterday quite directly said that we have to wait until the unpublished volumes are published and then somebody who has been elevated in a certain place will be found to be embarrassing in certain quarters. He had earlier used the terms 'elevated'--
Mr Hayden-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. It is a simple matter for the Prime Minister to have before him page 1085, column 1, paragraph 1 of the Hansard record of yesterday's debate. There is no reason or justification for his misstating facts.
Mr SPEAKER-The Leader of the Opposition will resume his seat. The Leader of the Opposition is not taking a point of order. I call the Prime Minister.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Mr Speaker, the Leader of the Opposition is quite unable to take it and continually interrupts, as we know. Let me read the words so that honourable members can again see what implications are drawn from those words. He said:
What happens when we discover the identity of the three eminent 'gentlemen'-I use the word 'gentlemen' rather loosely-who, as their reward for services to society, have been elevated to the peerage of the realm, who are well known as being involved in organised crime and who are making huge fortunes in this country? I do not have to identify them. They are well known to the Press Gallery. They are well known to the police, who have conveyed their identities to me.

The Minister for Administrative Services had something to say about that today. The Leader of the Opposition went on to say:
The Government is stung to retort. Let it retort on this: When the two unpublished volumes of the Costigan Royal Commission report are produced there will be some very embarrassed people very near the Premier of Queenslandpeople who have been elevated-
Again, that word 'elevated'-
and who have waxed profitably as a result of the favour from the State Government in Queensland are some of the names we will hear about.
There is only one implication that journalists could fairly draw from that, and many of them did draw it, as the newspapers and media have indicated quite clearly through this morning.
Mr Mildren-But is it true?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Someone interjected 'But is it true'. That again is the nature of this Opposition. The Leader of the Opposition, in saying what he did and in allowing people to come to the conclusions that they did, was quite plainly smearing a whole class, and falsely smearing a whole class-the class of people who are knights of the realm, whether under the imperial honours list orunder the Order of Australia.
I come now to the letter Mr Costigan wrote to me this morning. It was as a result of a request from an officer of my Department in relation to the unpublished volumes, or for that matter the published volumes, of his report. The letter states:
My dear Prime Minister, I have been asked by an officer of your Department to indi-
cate whether in any of the confidential volumes of my reports there are mentioned any knights in Sydney, Melbourne or Brisbane or elsewhere.
As you will understand from my letter dated 6 September it is my firm view that the details of those reports should not be made public either by way of tabling in the Pariiament or by piecemeal disclosure. Consistent with that view I believe there are dangers in responding to individual requests of this kind.
Nonetheless I think it appropriate that I should indicate in this case that there are no such knights mentioned.
The abuse of Parliament by the Leader of the Opposition is disgraceful. He knew how the journalists, the Press Gallery, would draw implications from what he said yesterday. He allowed it to run. He allowed it to be reported. Now he says that what he said yesterday was false. The Leader of the Opposition is totally beneath contempt.

AUSTRALIAN EMBASSY IN BEIRUT
Mr BRADFIELD-I ask the Minister for Foreign Affairs: In view of the now changed situation in Lebanon and the welcome cessation of hostilities in Beirut, can the Minister provide this Parliament with details of any plans to reopen the Australian embassy in that city?
Mr STREET-The Government is keeping under review the question of reopening the Australian embassy in Beirut. The successful completion of the withdrawal of the Palestine Liberation Organisation from Beirut is certainly a major achievement and it will go a long way to restoring the effective authority of the Government of Lebanon. It will be appreciated by the House, though, that the Government would not wish to reopen the embassy in Beirut until the security situation there is acceptable. An officer of my Department visited Beirut last week. He has reported on that visit and hIs report currently is being examined.

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1163

Mr HAYDEN-If we can somehow bridge the diversion without my going over the points I was making, if other newspapers have a wrong lead or interpretation similar to that which I saw in the Australian. they too will get a correction, as the Australian did early this morning. However, there is no justification for any error. Apart from a clear reading of the parliamentary record putting beyond any dispute that there was no reference, implicit or explicit, to the Costigan report in the terms in which I discussed this matter, it was made quite abundantly clear at a Press conference I conducted at about 4.10 p.m. in my office in Parliament House at which a large number of media people were present. I do not intend to waste time on that silly sort of distraction with the Prime Minister who seeks to become the sort 'of quintessential man of public decency, a role the public would not recognise, I am sure, in all of this.
I want to go on to the statement of the Minister for Administrative Services in which he said a couple of things. He referred to a letter from, I think, the Police Commissioner quoting my statement about people who have been elevated to the peerage of the realm. In case there is a semantic quibble there, I say that it was clearly a mistake to refer to 'peerage of the realm'. That refers to people who have been appointed as barons or whatever. I meant to refer to knights of the realm. I do not want to waste time on that point but I clarify it in case there is a semantic quibble in that regard about which the police are waffling. I did not use the word 'briefing' in my statement. I said: 'They are well known to the police who have conveyed their identities to me'.
There are a few things that have to be said about this matter. The Minister for Administrative Services may recall with some embarrassment when I jolt his memory about a conversation that he and I had on the front bench on the Government side of this House after the dinner break one night in the last session. I will come back to that in a minute. It is declared that I have not been to the Australian Federal Police headquarters. I mention in passing that on more than one occasion senior police officers, and not just members of the Federal Police, have mentioned this matter to me. I think honourable members will recall my past association with the police force and the fact that I maintain links with people who when I served in the police force were junior but who now hold senior positions. I also have a fairly friendly relationship with interstate police officers at a fairly senior level. If there is any quibble as to whether I have been to the Federal Police headquarters here, I merely draw attention to a letter sent to me

on 7 July 1981 from the training division of the Federal Police. It was an invitation to dine informally with members of the Australian Federal Police officers course. Members of the Federal Police and of the State police forces were present on that occasion. I met a number of them. On that occasion, as had happened on earlier occasions elsewhere and as has happened on other occasions subsequently, this matter was raised with me.
Now, the Minister for Administrative Services pretends that there is oblivion in his mind in relation to this matter. I suggest that it exists in relation to many other matters; there would be no debate about that on either side of the Parliament but I put that matter to one side. I remind the Minister that one night in the last session in this Parliament I crossed the chamber and sat with him on the front bench. I said to him that senior officers of various police forces, including his own from time to time, had been mentioning to me the names of some fairly eminent titled people from the corporate sector who were involved in organised crime. I mentioned their names. I mentioned the three names which were given to me by the people I met at the Federal Police officers' dinner. I will tell the House what the Minister said. In relation to one of those names he said: 'I have heard about that person. We have kept an eye on him but we have not been able to get anything on him. We are very suspicious of him but it is very hard to pin him'. That is what the Minister said in this House. If the Minister quibbles about matters of this nature being raised, if he wants to condemn with contumely the use of anonymity, and I think anonymity is proper in these casesthe matter is well understood-let me remind him of an article in the Canberra Times of 10 May this year in which he was quoted directly. The article states:
Asked if there were 'respectable, reputable businessmen mixed up in organised crime in Australia,' Mr Newman said, 'Of course there are, and they have become so entrenched they have organised themselves so that they are well safeguarded from the normal thing we have been used to in this country . . .
I do not think that the pot ought to be hurling bricks anywhere.

UNEMPLOYMENT
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Government's out-of-hand and counter-productive rejection of the ACTU proposals for attempting constructively to address the problem of unemployment in this country.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr HAWKE (Wills) (2.59)-Mr Speaker, in the atmosphere of last week's growing public outcry against the Government's reprehensible conduct in respect of the tax avoidance industry, which had been so starkly revealed in the fourth interim report of Commissioner Costigan, there may have been a tendency to overlook another disastrous illustration of why the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Government are no longer entitled to the respect and confidence of the people of this country. On Thursday and Friday of last week the Australian Council of Trade Unions held a federal unions conference, and out of that two-day conference there emerged a resolution of historic significance. I remind the House that such a conference of federal trade unions conducted by the ACTU between biennial congresses is the most representative way of testing the will of the trade union movement in this country. Out of that conference there emerged a resolution which I say is deserving of earnest consideration by the Government. I ask members of the House to bear in mind precisely what was said in that resolution which, following the federal unions conference was endorsed by the Executive of the ACTU. In particular I ask that the first paragraph of the resolution be borne clearly in mind. The trade union movement of Australia, so assembled, stated:
The causes of unemployment are manifold and as recognised in policy are not capable of simple solution. The following proposal does not pretend to be a panacea for all our economic problems nor will it create by itself full employment.
Right at the outset of that resolution which was passed by the federal unions conference and subsequently endorsed that day by the Federal Executive of the ACTU was a responsible statement starting off with the proposition that the causes of unemployment are manifold; that the

ACTU did not have all the answers and that it did not pretend to put forward a panacea in regard to this matter. Let me read further from what the ACTU, so assembled, said in respect of this fundamentally important matter. It also acknowledged in its resolution the significance in part of some things that are contained in the Budget. In other words, it was not simply trying to condemn the Federal Government totally out of hand, saying that nothing of relevance had been done. Indeed, the resolution stated:
The income tax changes announced in the 1982-83 Budget satisfy the wage and salary earners demand for tax indexation and as such they alleviate the need for unions to build income tax increases into claims for cost of living adjustments.
Again that is a thoroughly responsible and constructive approach by that assembling of the Australian trade union movement. It recognised that what happened in the Budget in respect of tax indexation met that part of its claim. As I said at the outset, there is first of all a responsible recognition that the trade union movement did not have all the answers and that the causes of unemployment were complex. Secondly, the ACTU pointed out that it took positive account of what the Government had done in the Budget. It went on to make very specific proposals about what could be done, on its part as well as by the Government, co-operatively, to try to deal with the problems confronting this country in general and in respect of unemployment in particular. It said:
We do not believe that the trade union movement can be expected to make the only contribution and for this reason there should be a collective response involving: (a) forgoing half the benefits of tax indexation (which means $4 per week at average weekly earnings level).
The ACTU went on to refer to the other matters which would be involved in such a comprehensive package. I again remind you, Mr Speaker, and the House of the context. The ACTU asserted that it did not have all the answers and that the causes of unemployment were complex; it recognised positively the contribution that the Budget has made in respect of tax indexation-that that had met the ACTU's part of the negotiations that it had had with the Government-and from that totally positive and responsible approach it went on to make a specific proposal about how the ACTU and the rest of the community could make a sacrifice by forgoing half the amount of tax indexation to create a national employment fund which would be directed, on the part of the ACTU which had made the sacrifice and the re.st of the community, to helping those in the community who were currently unemployed. That was the decision-an historic decision-that was taken by the ACTU on Friday of last week. The

Unemployment

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1167

whole context of the debate and the resolution showed on the part of the ACTU realism, responsibility and flexibility.
What was the response by this Government to that decision and attitude of the ACTU? The most convenient way of referring to the expressed reaction of the Government is to go to the Age of last Saturday. Under the heading 'Fraser scorns unions' job deal' we have a report on the reaction of the Government. The article stated:
The ACTU's offer to give up half the Budget tax cuts in return for a big job creation scheme was rejected by the Federal Government yesterday, only hours after it was approved by more than 150 unions.
Let us look at the very specific language of the rejection made by this Government. The article stated:
The acting Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations, Mr Brown, described the plan as a 'mild form of lunacy', hare-brained and absurd.
This was the reaction of the then Acting Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations to this positive proposal involving a sacrifice of half of the indexation cuts. The response by the Acting Minister was that the plan was a mild form of lunacy, hare-brained and absurd. If we are properly to understand the hypocrisy and the recklessness of this Government do not let me immediately rely on my saying on behalf of the Opposition what judgments should be made about this sort of response by the Gov\!rnment. Let me briefly refer to the range of editorial reaction to this response of the Government. Firstly, I go to the Australian Financial Review of Monday, 6 September. It stated:
Instead of attacking the employment creation plan put forward by the Australian Council of Trade Unions last week it would do the Government and others more credit if they were prepared to realise how far the majority of uniolls in the ACTU have moved towards a rational approach to Australia's economic problems.
The article went on:
It is one of the great political faults of the Prime Minister, Mr Fraser, that his instinct to kick a political head when he sees it prevents him from realising how far the ACTU has in fact moved towards genuine social compromise in the scheme put forward last week.
With an election looming in the not too distant future it is probably not surprising that the Prime Minister should not be willing to concede that the ACTU has travelled a long way along the path of reasonable compromise . . . If the Prime Minister were genuinely interested in consensus in the matter of wages policy he ought to recognise how far the ACTU has moved.
Let us look at the Canberra Times of the same date. What was its reaction to the plan? It stated:

The Government has chosen to characterise it as "lunacy", and to reiterate that wage increases are the root cause of unemployment. That is the sort of simplistic rhetoric that gets us nowhere.
The article went on to state:
- . . it has been hasty in dismissing it out of hand. That looks like a slap in the face to people prepared to drop their customary, hard adversarial role and try for real negotiation. It could lead to a very damaging standoff.
The Canberra Times said that the Government should accept:
- . . that the ACTU has offered a genu 'y useful set of proposals for negotiation . . .
We come to what yesterday's Age had to say. It stated:
The insulting way in which the Fraser Government rejected the ACTU's economic plan on Friday permits only one conclusion: that the Government is now more concerned about election tactics than economic management . . . There has been a growing recognition on all sides that Australia must learn from the consensus policies of the countries that have weathered the recession best . . .
In these circumstances, one would have thought the Government would recognise-as anyone concerned with the issue must recognise-that the ACTU's proposal was a very bold step towards such a consensus . . . This is a big and courageous step for the ACTU to make, and it deserved a warm reception from the Government . . . The Acting Minister, Mr Brown, consulted Mr Fraser, who saw it as merely a threat to the Government's campaign strategy for an early election.
The Age went on to say:
On his leader's instructions, Mr Brown not only rejected the offer, but did so in such a derisory manner as to suggest to the ACTU that it should not bother in future.
The editorial continued:
This is an abysmal way tomn an economy.
It concluded:
- . . the Fraser Government has slammed the door on the union moderates seeking consultation. By virtually telling them not to come back, it has ;.ven carte blanche to unions with industrial muscle to get what they can, while those lacking muscle fall behind.
In respect of the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee), who is at the table, the Age had this to say:
The unfortunate Mr Macphee may as well extend his holidays. There seems little point in negotiating an agreement when your principal wants a confrontation.
There is the wide range of reaction from the Australian Financial Review, the Canberra Times and the Age condemning the Government. What they said and indeed what we say here is not that the ACTU proposals are necessarily perfect and without the possibility of criticism, nor is it suggested that the ACTU would make that claim itself. What was universally recognised in those editorials was that here was a really constructive, positive approach along the road to consensus, a

1168 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Unemployment

recognition on behalf of the trade union movement that wages can play some part in the creation of economic problems including unemployment. They recognised on the part of the trade union movement an openness, a frankness, a holding out of the hand and saying: 'Look, we know that what we do can have some impact upon what happens to other people. It can have an adverse impact. What are we doing? We are acknowledging that and acknowledging that part of the Government's Budget was helpful in regard to the tax indexation claim. We are positively saying that we are prepared to forgo a half of the tax indexation that has been offered. Why? We believe that in that way we, as a trade union movement, can try constructively to do something to help those who are unemployed'. What do the unions get for their pains? They get this condemnation that they are engaged in lunacy and harebrained approaches. Is it any wonder that the Age, the Australian Financial Review and the Canberra Times condemn this Government and say that it was putting electoral advantage and perceived political advantage in front of the proper management and conduct of industrial relations of this country?
I say to my friend the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) at the table-I use those words 'my friend' advisedlythat the time has come when he must stand up and be counted on this issue. Is he going to repudiate publicly this offensive nonsense which, by general consensus, is seen to run counter to the best interests of this country? I simply cannot believe that the Minister endorses the approach of the Prime Minister and of the Minister for Communications, who on this occasion readily filled the role of malleable monkey for the organ grinder from Nareen. We had hoped that those sorts of days were behind us when the honourable member for Stirling (Mr Viner) was dismissed from this post. We have a tragedy in this country of high and rising unemployment which is an economic and a social disaster. The trade union movement, not necessarily with the perfect approach, has held out its hand in constructive cooperation. It is prepared to forgo half its tax indexation increases. The very least the Government should have done was to say: 'We welcome this constructive approach. We do not agree with all that you are putting, but we are prepared, in the interests of this country, to sit down and talk with you'.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations) (3.12)-The relationship between the Government and the Australian Council of Trade Unions just cannot be looked at in isolation in terms of the events of one week. I remind the House of the fact that on 7 July a tripartite conference was held at the request of the ACTU, agreed to by the Government with the employers, to consider what ought to be in the forthcoming Budget and what the interests of both sides of the industrial fence were. The ACTU put some very elaborate and certainly very constructive propositions to us. It actually made public a resume of what had been put on that occasion. In fact, if one drew up a table of what it put and what we have responded to in this Budget one would find that we have actually met all of its principal demands. Its demands did not contain a reference to the kind of so-called national employment fund which it announced last Friday.
Mr Hawke-The Budget did not contain the drought proposal either. You are not prepared to adapt to changing circumstances?
Mr MACPHEE-I am merely trying to be kind to the honourable member for Wills on this occasion. Clearly the drought proposals are the result of a worsening climatic situation; regrettably the unemployment situation has been with us for some time. We on the Government side have said that we do not in any way welcome the high levels of unemployment. We are doing what we can. We have said in many debates in this House, including one a couple of weeks ago, that we are trying to contain and reduce those high levels of unemployment. We sought the support of the ACTU in those measures. The ACTU had two opportunities before the BUdget-the last of them was on 7 july-to put views about employment and unemployment. It did not do so.
What has to be recognised is that the proposition now put by the ACTU, while it has the commendable features that the honourable member for Wills has pointed out, also has the commendable feature that the trade union movement of this country now acknowledges the importance of helping to curb unemployment and create employment. It also acknowledges that while it has not got all the answers and certainly is not fully responsible, it does bear some responsibility for improving that picture. It has that advantage and I can acknowledge that.
Mr Hawke-Do you repudiate what your Acting Minister said?
Mr MACPHEE-I will come to that in a moment. The practical observation which the

Unemployment

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1169

honourable member for Wills chose to ignore is: How does one, three weeks after the Budget is presented, respond to that kind of argument? Is the ACTU, for example, powerful enough to say to its members: 'Sign on the dotted line. Sign away half your tax reduction'? Are those 50 per cent or more of persons in the work force who are not unionised going to have the ACTU speaking for them? Will they say: 'Yes, we are quite happy because there has been a Federal unions meeting and someone has decided on our behalf that we will forgo our tax reductions in the Budget'? What the Acting Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr N. A. Brown) did say and what the honourable member chose to ignore was contained in the last paragraph of the Acting Minister's Press release. It stated:
If the ACTU wishes to have discussions with Government on the proposals it has put forward, then the Government is willing to do so. However, such discussions would need to be on the clear understanding of the importance the Government attaches to wage restraint in present economic circumstances.
The importance of that statement is that as a precondition, as it were, for this kind of national employment fund-a precondition for cooperation, so it seems, reading the ACTU's release-there was a commitment sought from the Government to support the maintenance of real wages. There were several references on the wages policy side to a precondition that the Government must agree to a centralised system of wage fixation which guaranteed indexation of wages. The Government made it clear that in the current economic circumstances that was not on. While I again congratulate the ACTU on getting this far, nevertheless one sees a great deal of vagueness in this quite extensive document. In fact, I have spoken by telephone to the President of the ACTU and said that when he thinks it is useful I would be pleased to have a yarn with him and any of his officials on what the plan means. We are agreed that the most useful thing for him to do now is to go, as he was asked to do by the meeting of last Friday, to the peak councils in the States-the trades and labour councils-and when he has completed that exercise we will have a discussion on what the document means.
I do not presume to quote the President but I think he would acknowledge the difficulty as is illustrated in that famous expression about the ink not being dry. When the Budget has been presented, what the ACTU has now put could not reasonably be entertained by the Government. It is hardly surprising that the recommendations put forward, both administratively and in terms of the principle of the Budget, cannot be accepted. But it is significant that, however belatedly the trade union movement generally, as distinct from some

of its leaders, now acknowledges the seriousness of unemployment, the fact is that to some extent that situation is worsened and has been brought about by some of the actions of some of the militant trade union leaders in respect of excessive wage demands and hours reductions. It is a matter about which they could be concerned and should be concerned.
It is very important too to note that the national employment fund, even if it were feasible to administer, would amount to an extra cost of some $785m. Admittedly this is to be taken away from certain other taxpayers who would believe they need it and whom we believe will use it effectively. The Senior Vice-President of the ACTU puts the number of persons who might be trained under the scheme at only some 40,000 in the next six months and perhaps as many as 120,000 within a year. No government can seriously consider in isolation that sort of proposition as opposed to the care which went into the preparation of this Budget which is aimed, of course, at helping to retain present levels of employment, improve the real disposable income of people and in fact to restore our economic growth.
The ACTU has at last recognised the unemployment situation. Let us not forget that the Australian Labor Party overlooked unemployment in the preparation of its policy for its biennial congress. In fact, both sides of the labour movement have been rather remiss on the question of unemployment whereas the Government has consistently expended increasing sums Budget by Budget on training programs designed to give people a level of skill for permanent employment. It is also worth noting that the Victorian Premier is now reported to be seeking to break some election commitments that he made on employment subsidisation. Whilst I do not know whether he has made a firm statement on this matter, there are reports in today's Press indicating that he is interested in breaking that election commitment. One of the most important aspects of that is that glib statements are made sometimes by trade union officials and Labor politicians about the gravity of unemployment and the fact that somehow or other the Government has to produce a magical answer. I think the ACTU and the Opposition have to recognise that the time for putting these views forward is prior to the preparation of the Budget. Having said that, I certainly will look forward to a detailed discussion with the ACTU on its proposals, to try to put some flesh on the bones and to see how relevant they might be in the preparation of the next year's Budget.

1170 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Unemployment

There is no doubt that the trade union movement has come a long way in recognising the unemployment situation and the fact that there is a link between excessive wage increases or labour costs and unemployment. At this stage I believe it would be irresponsible for us to support the proposition for indexation of real wages. I believe that the ACTU and its members should look ai the benefits that this Budget brings to them on 1 November. They should then make an assessment of what they believe might be done in the next year's Budget. No proposal of this nature was put to us in the tripartite discussions. I do not think that any reasonable person, including the editorialists, can expect the Government now, three weeks after the presentation of the Budget, to respond to a proposition of that nature.
If there was to be a federal union meeting one would have hoped it would have been before the tripartite conference. The date for the tripartite conference was set a good month before it was held and it was set to suit the convenience of the ACTU leadership. The statement by the ACTU on the mechanics of the fund to which reference has been made~indeed, it seems to me that the honourable member for Wills relied solely upon that fund~do not provide detail. They would not provide detail if he were in government trying to administer the fund. I have asked the ACTU when we do have our discussions to spell out what it wants to operate. Certainly as I read it now the Government would have to say to pay-as-youearn taxpayers 'We are going to change our mind
and call back the PAYE scale to take effect on I November'. This would be costly. It would be time consuming. It would mean that many people who are not affiliated with the ACTU and who would not want the ACTU speaking for them would say: 'Wait a minute, the Government is in breach of faith as far as I am concerned'.
The whole proposition is just too silly. The timing is quite absurd. I suggest that the wrath, or sense of frustration, which was felt by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Acting Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations owed itself to that. For the first time, a constructive tripartite discussion was held. All three parties told the Press how constructive and useful it was. The Prime Minister said that he and his Ministers took on board what the ACTU had to say and that its views would be reflected in the Budget. They were very clearly reflected in the Budget. On the night the Budget was brought down it was described as horrible. Three days later the ACTU had to put out a statement which showed, as the honourable member for Wills has acknowledged today, that the Budget had a

number of good features which met things its members were concerned about. It had many good features and very few irritating features the more that members of the ACTU took the time to examine it.
At last Friday's meeting the ACTU produced some idea of a new employment creation fund. That idea was not advanced to us before the Budget, and it is clearly something which requires Budget implementation. It would require a complete rewriting of this Budget if we were to try to cope with it now. The idea has been thought through no better than some of the plans that have come from the ALP over the years for the creation of jobs by subsidisation, without any certainty that people would be employed for any real length of time and not at the expense of other people. No matter how one looks at this proposal, there are many more questions to be asked than are answered in the ACTU's document. I do not believe that it will be able to provide within the next few months a detailed program which would be capable of implementation. I do not believe, in other words, that had the ACTU put the idea to us on 7 July we could have got it into the sort of shape required in time for this Budget. I welcome the fact that the ACTU is concerned at last about the relationship between wage increases and unemployment.
Mr Charles~When is the Government going to do something about unemployment? It has had seven years.
Mr MACPHEE~This Government, through its policies of economic management, which have been referred to in many debates in this House, in terms of employment creation and training for permanent employment has done more than any other government.
Mr Hawke~ You have reduced the funds by 35 per cent in real terms.
Mr MACPHEE~That is not so. There has been a dramatic increase in the funds available for all training and employment programs. I look forward to a serious discussion with the ACTU when it has completed discussions with its trades hall councils. We will then do our best to try to accommodate its latest idea in a practical way in next year's Budget.
Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (3.27)~ln seconding the discussion of the matter of public importance brought forward by the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke), it is interesting to note that at last we have had a statement, or at least a few words, from the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) on the subject of employment. There is not much

Unemployment

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1171

doubt from what he said that he does not agree with the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown), who was acting in his place while he was away having a holiday. To put this matter into perspective, we need to look at some of the things that have happened since the Budget. On 24 August the Prime Minister was asked this question in this House in relation to the Budget:
What does the Budget do that will provide incentives to those making claims for increases in wages, particularly unions, to diminish those claims?
The Prime Minister said:
There is a very substantial incentive in the Budget to moderate wage claims as a result of the totality of the measures that the Government introduced.
The honourable member for Wills indicated that the trade union movement in deliberations at its conference recognised that some of the things that were done in the Budget were of benefit to the union movement. The Prime Minister also said:
I believe it is reasonable, and the Australian Council of Trade Unions has recognised that it is reasonable, to think that this establishes a precondition for wage restraint through the course of this year.
That was said on 24 August, and I started to believe that perhaps the Prime Minister was learning that a consensus approach could work. However, it did not last long. As the honourable member for Wills clearly indicated, on 3 September when the ACTU approached the Government on this matter there was an immediate reaction. The confrontation approach took over while the Minister was absent and another Minister was taking his place. Clearly, the Prime Minister scorned the unions job deal. The Acting Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations described it as a mild form of lunacy, as the honourable member for Wills has said. The immediate reaction of this Government to a genuine attempt to do something about employment problems in this country was to attack it on a confrontation basis. From a few days after the Budget when there was a slight softening of the confrontation approach we went back into it full bore, while the Minister was away.
We are putting forward today the argument that there was an out-of-hand rejection. The honourable member for Wills has clearly indicated that. The statements that were made and the way in which they were reported in the Press were clearly seen by the public and the media in this country as indicating that a sensible proposal put forward for discussion had been rejected out of hand. It was a counter-productive rejection. Either we have a common sense approach in this country in negotiations on wages

and conditions and dealings between governments and unions or we continue with the sorts of confrontation policies we have had for the last seven years, and which are counter-productive. They will not do anything for the stability of the economy in this country. They will not do anything for the problems of unemployment in respect of which the ACTU was trying to put forward proposals. The Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations, who is at the table, has told us in statements on the Budget that we can expect unemployment to rise next year to 8 per cent, an increase of 40 per cent to 50 per cent over this year. He announced those figures in speeches following the Budget but when the trade union movement comes together and presents a plan for discussion the Government's approach is a confrontationist counter-productive rejection of the proposal.
The problems of unemployment will not go away. The Minister said that a tripartite conference was held on 7 July so that the unions could sit down with the Government and put their claims before the Budget was brought down. The Government claims that it met the princpal demands, but it completely ignored any proposals on job creation. The Budget has clearly indicated that people will be out of work in greater numbers than ever before. The Government has come forward with no proposals for jobs. It claims that it is doing things for job training. There is not much point in the Government's putting money into job training when it is not prepared to put something towards creating jobs. The trade union movement made a genuine attempt. The union came together and said to the Government: 'We are prepared to put forward a plan whereby $725m of the tax indexation concessions can be used to create employment, to establish a national employment fund.' The Government is completely ignoring that proposal. It is using the work force as a tool for inflation control and doing nothing to counter the effects of unemployment on the families of this nation.
Let me get back to the matter of vital importance. The Minister first of all acknowledges that the proposals have some merit. He congratulated the ACTU on the document. He told us that he has spoken to the ACTU President and that he is prepared to negotiate and talk about employment problems with the trade union movement. What a complete opposite is the Minister's attitude to the initial reaction by the Prime Minister and the Acting Minister, who went to the Prime Minister and got the okay to issue his statement to the Press. Fancy any Minister, the day after a proposition is put forward to a government, saying that it is a

mild form of lunacy, hare-brained and absurd! At the end of his Press release the Minister said that perhaps the Government could talk about it. This is the type of approach we have seen for seven years.
It will never do anything for the economy of Australia. If we really want to see the sorts of problems this has created in the past and will create in the future, particularly for this Minister, we only have only to go back to 28 April 1981 and the resignation of the former Minister for Industrial Relations, the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock). cent of the work force this year, are waiting for this Government to come up with a policy. The trade union movement has come forward with a sensible proposal. We have heard the Minister say that he is prepared to talk. We have heard the Prime Minister and the Acting Minister clearly scorn what the trade union movement did. This Government has to make up its mind what its policy on the unemployed is. Does it want confrontation and more problems or will it take a consensus line and talk sensibly to the union movement about the massive problems that come to any country which has 8 per cent unemployment and half a million people who cannot receive ajob or a wage?

Further, whilst I recognise the need to create equity in our taxation system by removing the practice of tax avoidance and evasion, I challenge

completely the ACTU's reference and the Opposition's belief that this transfer of funds from the private sector to the public sector will create extra jobs; nor will any proposal such as the re-establishment of free health services or expanded welfare programs. Money must be provided from somewhere and it is time that the ACTU discovered and informed its members that they-the members-are and will remain the principal source of government revenue, whatever the political colour of the government. We will not improve the standard of living by providing a free service and then reducing wages through tax, and Opposition members can laugh at that. The
ACTU and its past President seem committed to government job creation programs. The past President, of course, tried some direct input during his presidency of the ACTU. I commend him for trying. The notable attempt, of course, was the ACTU's takeover of Bourke's in Melbourne, a veritable workers' co-operative where the loyal union members would flock in their thousands thus improving their standard of living and creatingjobs.

Petitions

have the same compulsion he had with union membership. Let us get down to the point and recognise the simple fact of prosperity. Prosperity was explained by two men who were discussing a successful and self-made businessman. One said: 'Ah, but he has been lucky.' The other said: 'Yes, and the harder he works the luckier he seems to get'. In those words lies the solution to unemployment in Australia. The solution does not lie in reduced wages, higher taxes, free medicine or higher unemployment payments, as proposed in the ACTU document. It lies in increased productivity per dollar paid. It lies in increased hours worked and, in particular, in work carried out for five days a week uninterrupted by strikes. It lies in more production and more exports.
The ACTU can make suggestions about Government policy and at many times it has been welcome to do so. Its responsibility, nevertheless, is to get its own house in order first. Were the ACTU prepared to go down the genuine jobcreation road instead of the Government-bashing road it might discover some of the following information. Yesterday the world spent $40 billion. Yesterday Australia spent $400m, about one per cent of that total world figure. Australia shares only one per cent of world trade and a very much smaller percentage of the labour intensive manufacturing sector. One need only refer to the standards of living that applied in Australia before its present involvement in international trade to realise that world trade is the basis for all employment in Australia today. That being the case, more than 6 million Australians gain employment from one per cent of world trade. As unemployment in Australia now stands below 7 per cent it can be said that an additional 0.07 per cent of world trade could remove all unemployment costs from the Australian taxpayer. Such a small percentage could hardly be measured, even in the present downturn of world trade, and is obviously achievable. It just requires that people get together as Australians and not suggest that one can take a dollar out of one pocket and put it in another pocket to create a job. That is all the ACTU proposal says. It is typical Labor Party economics and it is not worth two bob. Any time we like to look around we can see jobs going out the window because nobody has ever sat down--
