SPECIAL PROSECUTORS BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr N. A. Brown, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications and Minister Assisting the Attorney-General) (3.53)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.

The purpose of this Bill is to enable appointments to be made of Special Prosecutors with clear authority and independence to institute in exceptional cases and carryon necessary prosecutions. These cases will be of such a special character that the community should not be left in the slightest doubt that prosecutions will be handled with the highest professional competence with complete independence from any extraneous consideration. The first appointment of a Special Prosecutor will be of a person to assume overall responsibility for the handling of the prosecutions for the alleged taxation frauds referred to as bottom-of-the-harbour schemes and for coordinating the recovery by civil remedies under existing law of the amounts thereby lost to the Commonwealth. This legislation is therefore a clear indication of the Government's intention to proceed with the utmost vigour in these cases regardless of all considerations other than the proper enforcement of the law and the protection of the revenue.
The principal task of a Special Prosecutor will be in relation to breaches of laws of the Commonwealth or a Territory but he will be able to conduct prosecutions for offences against the laws of a State where he receives an appropriate appointment by or under the law of the relevant State. The Government is determined that the Special Prosecutor be backed and supported by all necessary staff including lawyers, accountants and investigating staff and special provision is made in clauses 17, 18 and 19 to ensure this result. The accountability of a Special Prosecutor is ensured by the requirement for annual reports of his operations. His independence is assured by the provisions of clause 7 limiting any direction or guidelines to directions or guidelines of a general character not relating to a particular case. Special Prosecutors appointed under the legislation will play an important role in the administration of criminal justice under Commonwealth law within the limits that will be imposed on them by the Attorney-General's delineation of their functions.
Debate (on motion by Mr Young) adjourned.
SALES TAX AMENDMENT BILLS (Nos 1 to 9) 1982
Second Readings
Debate resumed from 17 August, on motion by MrHoward:
That the Bills be now read a second time.
Sir JAMES KILLEN (Moreton-Leader of the House)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may 1 have your indulgence to suggest the House have a general debate covering these Bills and the Sales Tax

1178 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

(Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill 1982 as they are associated measures. Separate questions will, of course, be put on each of the Bills at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these measures? There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (4.56)-The Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos I to 9) 1982 increase sales tax rates by 2t percentage points; that is, the three categories of sales tax rates are each increased by 2t percentage points. The lowest tax rate is increased from 5 per cent to 7t per cent; the intermediate rate, from 17t per cent to 20 per cent; and the top rate, from 30 per cent to 32t per cent. This is the second year in a row in which there have been significant increases in the rates of sales tax. Last year also sales tax rates increased by 2t percentage points, which means that the rate of tax on goods in the third Schedule, which incurs tax at the lowest rate, has gone up from 2t per cent to 7t per cent in the last two Budgets covering a period of just one year. A trebling of rates in a year certainly is a very substantial rate of increase.
Goods covered in the lowest tax schedule which has had this enormous rate of increase in the rate of tax are principally household furniture and appliances and other household goods. Goods taxable at the intermediate rate have had their tax rate increased by one-third in the last two Budgets; that is, from 15 per cent to 20 per cent in the space of one year. These goods include cars, commercial vehicles, boats, computers, confectionery, stationery, soft drinks, spirits, beer and imported wine. The goods taxable at the top rate have had their tax increased by almost one-fifth in the space of one year; that is, over the last two Budgets. They include such items as radios, televisions, cameras, jewellery, cosmetics and so on.
The Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill removes exemptions from a range of goods and imposes tax on them at 7t per cent. The goods not exempt, but which will now be taxed, include maps; hand tools, including gardening tools; motion picture films; household drapery and soft furnishings, including sheets and towels; servicing and construction equipment; cleaning cloths; and blankets. All those items were not previously subject to sales tax but are to be taxed at 7t per cent from I January next year. Some of those goods which are now to become taxable were included in the range of goods the Government sought to remove exemption from last year. The House will recall that in last year's

Budget an attempt was made to raise $330m in a full year by taxing a whole array of items which were previously not taxable. The Government has not sought to reintroduce such an attempt to tax all those items again this year, but some of those items have been included in the list of goods which are now to be made taxable. Of course, last year the Government dropped its attempts to spread that tax to the wide variety of goods which had previously been exempt. This year it has picked up a number of items at which it was looking last year, including soft furnishings, drapery, manchester and hand tools.
These Bills amount to yet another move by the Government towards the introduction of a broadly based tax on goods and services. Over the last few years the Government has taken various measures to try to achieve this objective. In 1978 it set up an inquiry into such taxes as a broad based tax on goods and services, which inquiry was soon abandoned following a major campaign by the Retail Traders Association. The Government set up another similar inquiry in 1980 following the 1980 election, which inquiry recommended an extended wholesale sales tax, which the Treasurer (Mr Howard) again rejected under political pressure by early 1981. In last year's Budget the Government extended the wholesale sales tax. The Treasurer denied that this was a move towards a more broadly based system and described it only as a small adjustment within the existing system.
Had all those intended changes in last year's Budget in respect of sales tax been passed the fullyear revenue effect would have been a 32.7 per cent increase over the previous year's collections. It would have been a very substantial rate of increase in sales tax revenues indeed-hardly one, I would have thought, which befits the description of adjustment within the existing system. As I have previously mentioned, the attempts to obtain that substantial increase failed principally because the extension of tax to items previously exempt was abandoned eventually by the Government.
Fin:' lly, of course, the Government has announced further extensions in this year's Budget equivalent to a 20.8 per cent increase over last year's collections. So we have that substantial increase in sales tax revenue this year. Many new goods have been brought into the sales tax system by the correction of so-called anomalies and the extensions to previously exempt goods in this year's Budget. This is little more than a gradual move to a broader-based tax by the back door, which we can expect to see more of in future

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1179

Budgets should this Government remain in office-which seems very doubtful.
These increases mean that sales tax collections have risen from 6+ per cent of total Commonwealth tax collections in 1980-81 to 8.7 per cent this year, which is a very substantial leap in just two years. This has happened because of the very substantial sales tax measures which were announced in last year's Budget and in this year's Budget. As I have said, all this indicates attempts by the Government to introduce a broad-based indirect tax system in this country-a substantial move towards indirect taxes and less reliance perhaps on income tax. The Government may talk of less reliance on income tax but there has not been much evidence of that until this year's Budget and even now the tax cuts are small indeed.
The Opposition is not in favour of these measures but will not seek to prevent their passage by the Parliament. However, we wish to give formal expression to our concern at these measures. I therefore move the following amendment:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing the Bills the House expresses its concern at the Government's actions in again increasing sales taxes, which increases will have adverse consequences in regard to inflation, the equity of the tax system, and living standards'.
Let me amplify our reasons for concern at the imposition of these tax increases. As the amendment moved at the second reading stage states, we are concerned about the inflationary impact of what the Government is doing. There is no doubt whatever that these measures add to the rate of inflation. The Treasurer admitted that. He said in this House in March 1981 that a $500m increase in indirect taxes represents a one per cent increase in the consumer price index. He admitted quite clearly that substantial moves towards reliance on indirect taxes increases the rate of inflation and pushes up the consumer price index at that rateone per cent for an increase of$500m. By virtue of the Bills now before us, the government is seeking to increase sales tax revenue this financial year by $414m and by $595m in a full year. The full year effect-the figure one should look at if one is concerned about prices-is over $500m. We can therefore say that the Treasurer, on the basis of his own calculations given to this House, is directly and indirectly adding something like one per cent or more to the consumer price index by increasing these sales tax rates and removing exemptions. That is clearly what he is doing.
This is a government which we are told fights inflation first, but last year and this year it sought

to increase sales taxes in a way which clearly adds to inflation. The Government is fighting inflation by increasing prices. That is a very curious process indeed. This increase of one per cent from the increase in sales taxes in this Budget is not the end of the story. Other increases in indirect taxes in this Budget will certainly mean that in total the increase in the consumer price index, which comes from this move to increase indirect taxes, will be over 2 per cent. The total increase in direct taxes from measures announced in this Budget will be $I,046m this year but in a full year it will be $I,445m. That $1,445m, it can be seen, will add something near to 3 per cent to the consumer price index, on the basis that $500m equals one percent.
This Budget is supposed to be concerned, as all previous Fraser Government Budgets were concerned, with fighting inflation, but it is quite clearly, on the face of the increases in revenue and the calculations given by the Treasurer, directly adding nearly 3 per cent to inflation by the increases in indirect taxes contained in it. That strikes us as quite an incredible process. Clearly it means a very significant boost to inflation. It is of course a major reason for spiralling unemployment. The rate of unemployment is now nearly 7 per cent. The Government admitted that in the very near future it will be over 8 per cent, and the Australian Financial Review claimed that by the end of this financial year it will be 10 per cent. Despite spiralling unemployment we still have a rate of inflation of the order of 11 per cent, on the Government's own calculations. Of course, a major reason why it is still of that order is that the Government is directly adding nearly 3 per cent to the rate of inflation by measures such as the one now before the House.
If the Government were concerned about getting the level of inflation down, one would think it would not add 3 per cent to the inflation rate by its own actions, but that is what it will do. By increasing these taxes it quite clearly not only directly increases the level of the CPI but also stimulates income claims by wage earners and others to make up for that increase in prices, and therefore adds indirectly to inflation. So we have quite a substantial inflationary impact stemming from such action. Both directly and indirectly there are inflationary impacts which in the end cause this very high level of inflation to continue despite massive unemployment. It strikes us as quite an incredible economic policy to pursue inflationary measures at a time when the Government continues to say that it is concerned to fight inflation. I must say that in the last year or two that rhetoric has tended to break down a little.

J J80 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September J982

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

Last year's Budget raised indirect taxes almost wholly through sales tax rate increases by $449m in that financial year, and $596m in a full year. If we look at last year's Budget and this year's Budget we can say that in the space of a year-it is only just over a year ago since we discussed last year's Budget; indeed, we did so at this time last year-we had increases in indirect taxes of the order of $2 billion, or $2,041 m to be exact. Again, on the basis of the Treasurer's own calculations, that adds about 4 per cent to the inflation rate in the space of a year. That is the kind of inflationary impact that we see from the Government's own actions. It is indeed a very large influence on the inflation rate. If it were not occurring we could be pretty sure-in fact, we could be absolutely certain-that there would be a much lower inflation rate than at present.
I wish to make one other point in respect of the inflationary effect of sales tax increases, and that is that the increase in prices which occurs because of sales tax increases is not only the amount of the sales tax increase. This wholesale sales tax is placed on the last wholesale price, but between the wholesale price and the retail price there is a substantial mark up. The mark up includes the wholesale price which incorporates the amount of sales tax. When one increases the level of sales tax, with constant mark ups, there is an automatic increase in the price as a result of not only the increase in the sales tax but also the 50 per cent mark up or whatever it is. A 50 per cent mark up and a 2t per cent increase in the price of, say, a refrigerator as a result of this Budget means that the sales tax increase will also be marked up. The increase in the price of that refrigerator will be 3.75 per cent. With a 7t per cent tax applying to refrigerators, the actual increase in price caused by taxing refrigerators is not 7t per cent but II t per cent, assuming a 50 per cent retail mark up. That is something to bear in mind when we look at these increases. It is not just a 2t per cent increase in the price; it is a 2t per cent increase in the wholesale price which, with constant markups, means that the retail price will go up at a higher level than that. The Budget has accelerated the inflationary impact in that way.
Another factor which causes us concern about these measures is the regressive nature of indirect taxes. As I have shown in earlier debates, increases in indirect taxes bear most heavily on lower income groups. Increases in indirect taxes are likely to reduce the equity of the tax system. It certainly is true that various international studies have looked at indirect taxes in many countries to ascertain where they fall and who is most affected by them. Some have argued that where broad

based indirect taxes such as VAT-value added tax-apply the impact is near proportional, or at best proportional. I know of no one who argues that indirect taxes have a progressive effect in the way that income tax does if in fact the tax is not avoided by people on high incomes, as is certainly the case in this country at present. But certainly those international studies indicate that at best there is a proportional impact from indirect taxes and that most often the tax is regressive.
If that is the case in other countries we can certainly assume it to be the case here. Indeed, various studies have shown that that is the case here. I have alluded to those studies in previous debates in this House and I do not intend to refer to them now. We believe it is quite clear that the Australian indirect tax system is regressive. More reliance on that system means greater inequity in the tax system, or less equity, and that is a process which we deplore. In saying that I must take account of the fact that in this year's Budget the reductions in income tax that have been implemented-which cuts are in reality far less than the Government maintains after one takes account of the absence of tax indexation-are shaped in such a way that they do give greater benefits to lower income earners than to higher income earners, at least as regards the income tax scale. Items such as the mortgage interest tax deduction can be said to work in the opposite direction.
The countervailing increases in indirect taxes, much of which we are now discussing in the Bills before the House, will substantially, if not completely, offset the slight improvement in the equity of the tax system that comes from the revised income tax arrangements for this year. We do not see the tax system as having been improved in this Budget. Indeed, any benefits which have come from shaping the income tax cuts in a way which gives greater proportional benefits to people on lower incomes has been offset by the heavy additional increases in indirect taxes which are incorporated in the Budget.
Thirdly, dealing with the effect on living standards, the rise in sales taxes this year considerably reduces the improvement to living standards contained in other Budget measures. This financial year the average family will have its living standard reduced by $1.75 a week because of increased sales tax rates in the Budget. That figure comes from taking the total amount of revenue to be raised this year from sales tax increases and dividing that total by the number of Australian

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1181

households. We get about $1.75 a week in additional sales tax payments per Australian household this financial year. With other increases in indirect taxes there will be an impact of about $4.40 a week on the living standards of Australian households. That is, they will pay on average $4.40 a week more in indirect taxes this year than they did last year as a result of the increases in the tax rates and the abolition of some exemptions which are contained in the Budget. That increase in sales tax more than counteracts the benefits from this year's Budget for many families, indeed for most of them, as I pointed out in my speech on the Budget. I do not intend to go back over them.
Furthermore, the increases in indirect taxes in the last two Budgets, in association with the increase in the income tax burden that has occurred in those two Budgets taken together, have more than wiped out the benefits to living standards from other measures in this year's Budget for almost all households. We can say that over the last two years there has not been a reduction in income tax. Indeed, there has been an increase in the income tax burden for Australian households. There has also been a significant increase in the indirect tax burden on Australian households, and those increases in the direct and indirect tax burden quite clearly and markedly outweigh any benefits contained in this year's Budget by way of increased family allowances or other benefits. We see Australian family living standards as having been adversely affected by the increases in indirect taxes in last year's Budget and in this one.
There is a fourth area of concern about the measures now before the House. We cannot accept the Government's arguments for implementing these increases. The Government argues, for two reasons, that it is desirable to place greater reliance on indirect taxes and less on income tax than is currently the case. Firstly, the Government says that we raise an unduly high proportion of our total tax revenue from income tax and therefore we should move more to indirect taxes. Secondly, it says that greater reliance on indirect taxes would enable us to reduce our losses through tax avoidance and tax evasion. Those are the two key arguments which this Gov":', ,ment continually refers to as being reasons to move towards a broad based indirect tax system or more reliance on indirect taxes.
In regard to the first of those arguments, it is already the case that we raise a higher percentage of our taxes [[('IT' indirect taxes than do countries generally in llle Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development. That is, on average we raise a higher proportion of our taxes from indirect taxes than do Western capit:.llist countries.

The latest year for which we have comparable statistics is 1979. In that year Australia raised 31.71 per cent of its taxes from indirect taxes and the OECD average was 29.35 per cent. So in 1979 we were quite notably above the OECD average for the proportion of our taxes which we raised through indirect taxes. Since 1979 we have had big increases in the crude oil levy, and in the last two Budgets we have had big increases in sales taxes and other indirect taxes. We can assume, I think rightly, that that differential between us and the OECD would have increased over the three years since 1979. We believe that the argument that we need to move more towards indirect taxes because we have an undue reliance on income tax simply ignores the fact that Australia already has a relatively high proportion of its taxes raised through indirect taxes. The Government will simply not confront that argument.
The second argument which the Government uses is that more reliance on indirect taxes will help to reduce tax avoidance and tax evasion. I should like to make two points about that. It is just a straight cop-out to say that we cannot overcome the tax avoidance problem, therefore we will move more towards indirect taxes and make sure that those who are currently avoiding tax at least pay some tax through higher sales taxes. That is the kind of argument that is being used and it is one which we believe is totally and completely unacceptable. The duty of this Government is to make sure that people do not avoid income tax. Of course, as we have known from what has been discussed in this House in the last couple of weeks, and indeed over the last few years, this Government has been hopeless in preventing massive tax avoidance and evasion. Having come to the point almost of despair at its total inability to close off tax avoidance and evasion, or unwillingness to do anything about it, the Government says: 'Let us move more towards indirect taxes because at least in that way we will ensure that when people spend their money on various goods they will pay some taxes'. We believe that that is a complete cop-out of the Governments responsibilities. It is duty bound to ensure that it does everything in its power to prevent tax avoidance and evasion. I do not want to canvass the arguments in that regard now, but it is patently not doing that. It should be doing that if it wants to overcome the problem of tax avoidance, not use this cop-out approach and saying: 'Let us move towards indirect taxes so that the tax avoiders have to pay something when they buy a refrigerator, a blanket or whatever'.
The second reaction we have to the Government's argument is that it is simply not the case

1182 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

that indirect taxes are avoidance proof or anywhere near it. It is not the case overseas; it is not the case here. A report entitled 'Value Added Tax-a Study of its Practical Aspects' by 1. D. Petty, a research paper for the Taxation Institute of Australian Research and Education Trust, is a study of indirect taxes overseas. It shows quite clearly that in country after country-the United Kingdom, France, the Netherlands, Denmark and Italy-there has been great trouble in preventing avoidance of value-added tax. Value-added tax is supposed to be the most secure, most selfenforcing, most avoidance proof of all the indirect taxes. Yet here we have a study which points to all sorts of problems that have been faced by governments overseas with the value-added tax. If one looks at the overseas experience one certainly cannot get any satisfaction about indirect taxes being avoidance proof, and therefore if we go more towards indirect taxes we will overcome the problems of tax avoidance. We simply will do that, on the basis of overseas experience. In regard to our own experience there is no reason to believe that that would be the case.
The Asprey Taxation Review Committee, which reported in 1975, indicated a variety of ways in which sales taxes could be easily avoided. Recent annual reports of the Commissioner of Taxation have indicated a considerable degree of detected avoidance and evasion of sales taxes. The latest annual report of the Taxation Commissioner says that 20,206 investigations were conducted during 1980-81 resulting in additional tax and penalties of $20.6m. This is some evidence of avoidance and evasion. Also, the annual reports of the Taxation Commissioner show that the number of legal proceedings for enforcement of sales tax increased by 214 per cent between 1975-76 and 1979-80. In a very short period indeed we had a very substantial increase in the number of legal proceedings in this area. So, we can see that evidence shows that there is great difficulty in preventing avoidance of sales tax and indirect taxes.
But there has been also a lot of evidence recently about various new schemes which have been introduced in regard to avoidance of sales taxes. These take various forms. One form this avoidance takes is through price splitting which is used predominantly in the cosmetic, soft drink and pet food industries. Essentially, the price of a good is split into a manufacturing cost and a service cost with the service cost being funnelled through a subsidiary company set up for the purpose. The price claimed as the price of the product is taxable but the service charge is not. Cadbury Schweppes Pty Ltd is one company which

has reportedly set up a service company in February of this year. It began double invoicing customers for, on the one hand, a service charge and, on the other hand, the cost of soft drinks. There have been various other evidences in the daily Press of this and other forms of schemes. As recently as yesterday there was an article in the Sydney Sun which was headlined 'Dealers In $250 mil Car Tax Fraud'. What this article does is to expose the way in which car dealers are paying very little sales tax indeed on new cars. As that becomes more widely known the competitors are forced to get into the business otherwise they are undercut in their pricing and lose the business. So, we are seeing a mushrooming of that scheme which the Sydney Sun says is in the order of $250m. There is, quite clearly a lot of evidence of avoidance of sales taxes. It is a nonsense to say that this is an avoidance proof tax and that a movement in this area will mean less tax avoidance.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Jarman)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired. Is the amendment seconded?
Mr Young-I second the amendment and reserve my right to speak.
Mr PORTER (Barker) (4.28)-It never ceases to amaze me, following as I often do the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis), in speaking on economic subjects such as sales tax, the way he can pursue the figures to represent a case which I find extraordinarily difficult to believe. He suggested that Australia has a high level of indirect taxation compared with the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development countries. I just happen to have with me the statement that the Treasurer (Mr Howard) made on 12 March 1982. That statement shows clearly that the proportion of total tax revenue raised in Australia from general consumption taxes was 6.1 per cent. Out of 22 OECD countries we rank twenty-first. How on earth the honourable member can say then that we rank highly amongst the other OECD countries is absolutely beyond me. I suggest that the honourable member for Gellibrand refer to that speech and the tables 1 to 4 annexed to it which should clarify the matter for him.
This year's Budget increases both expenditure and receipts. Part of the increase in receipts is from the sales tax measures which are the subject of this debate. Before I discuss them in detail I will deal with some of the broader issues. Clearly, in a time of world and Australian economic recession the type of Budget a government brings down must have different goals from a Budget presented

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1183

in a relatively better economic climate. This Budget increases expenditure by 13.9 per cent and receipts by 11.3 per cent. That expenditure could well be increased further with the drought aid announcement made last week. That aid, of course, is meeting a specific need which has arisen in rural areas. Given the disastrous nature of the drought, its extent and its effects that aid was clearly called for, and I welcome the Government's initiative.
The point I make is that the increase in expenditure and the deficit is clearly aimed at stimulating the economy in this period of recession. I believe it is appropriate and defensible on those grounds. I say that because I am a strong believer in smaller government rather than larger government. I adhere to the view that we ought to be pressing to reduce the size of government. However, to adopt rigidly that line of argument while ignoring the recession and the appropriate remedy for our current economic ills would be unrealistically intransigent.
It will be well known that during the I930s recession governments generally undertook a great deal of belt-tightening which many believed aggravated the problems of that period. Having learned our lesson most economists agree that it is better now to try to mildly stimulate economic activities in times such as this, which, of course, is one of the objectives of this Budget. Australia is not only confronted with a world recession but also the recession comes at a time when, as a result of excessive wage demands, we have seriously disadvantaged ourselves because we have lost a great deal of our competitive advantage on world markets. It is, therefore, essential that our wages structure be brought into line with the rest of the world. This Budget provides enormous assistance through reductions in taxation and an increase in benefits to all Australians. The benefits especially for the average family are worth around $20 a week. Even if we deduct the cost of the increases on sales taxes imposed by these Bills it is well recognised that the average family will still be around $17 a week better off.
The Government has taken the initiative to increase take-home pay. That initiative must be taken into consideration when wage increases are being sought. It is in this context that I repeat: Our wage structure must be brought into line with the rest of the world. I am, therefore, disgusted when I see that, despite the Government's action and despite the high level of unemployment in Australia, the Australian Council of Trade Unions appears to remain intent on entrenching excessive levels of wages growth. I quote the Acting Minister for Employment and Industrial

Relations, the present Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown), who said on 3 September:
The ACTU's wages strategy, if put into effect, would mean a substantial further increase in real earnings over 1982-83, a further squeeze on profits and a marked deterioration in employment.
He went on:
This strategy involves a specific campaign of industrial action in sensitive industries in order to force employers to concede. The unions seem unwilling to bring their claims to the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission where they can be examined on their merits with regard to the public interest, free of disruptive industrial action.
If Australia is to progress out of this recession there must be co-operation from the unions, otherwise there will be higher unemployment and lower standards of living.
I turn now to the details of these Bills. The Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos I to 9) impose an increase on the various rates of sales tax of 2t percentage. In other words, the respective rates will increase from 5 per cent to 7t per cent, from 17t per cent to 20 per cent and from 30 per cent to 32t per cent. The result is an increase in revenue this year of $370m and, in a full year, an increase of $490m. The Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill imposes sales taxes of 7t per cent on a range of goods that were previously exempt. Those goods range from mats, hand tools and to equipment used in the course of servicing, repair or construction. It does not include goods which become part of the goods being repaired or the structure being built. The Bill will increase revenue by $44m this year and $105m in a full year. The total estimated revenue from these changes this year will be $414m.
Mr Robert Brown-Ripping off the poor.
Mr PORTER-In a full year this will amount to $595m. It is interesting to hear the interjection: 'Ripping off the poor'. I believe we ought to compare those increases with the expected cost to government revenue of some of the income tax reductions which are initiated in this Budget. Honourable members will see from the figures that the interjection is totally false. The Opposition seems blind to the real economic effects of this Budget. There are changes in the income tax rates scale and I will outline them: There is a reduction in the standard rate of tax from 32c down to 30c in the dollar; there is an increase in the tax free threshold from $4,195 to $4,595; and the standard rate step of tax will rise from $17,894 to $19,500. The cost for this year will be $1,468m. Let us compare that with the $414m increase in revenue from the sales tax measures, the subject of these Bills. It is quite clear that the thrust of the

1184 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

Budget is to increase take home pay. The interjections are totally false. The full year cost of the income tax reductions is $2,357m. Compare that with $595m, the full year cost of the sales tax measures, the subject of these Bills. There is no doubt that those figures support the view that families will be better off as a result of this Budget.
However, there is a further and important matter to be considered when discussing these Bills. They are a step in the right direction in correcting an imbalance in our reliance on income tax as a source of revenue. I will refer to the table which I referred to earlier in this debate, which is an annexure to the Treasurer's statement last year. One can see that 20 years ago, in 1962-63, the proportion of the Commonwealth Government's revenue raised from personal income tax was around 37 per cent. This year it is around 55 per cent. In other words, over the last 20 years there has been a steady growth in the proportion of revenue coming from income tax. I believe it is imperative to redress this situation because high levels of income tax are a disincentive for individuals. It is a disincentive to invest and a disincentive for individuals to increase their work effort or to do overtime. These are fundamental reasons why there are obvious advantages in changing the mix of direct and indirect taxation. Clearly, if we are to shift our emphasis of revenue raising away from direct taxation it will increase the prospect of further substantial and desirable reductions in direct tax, particularly personal income tax.
These Bills are effective in reducing the amount that would otherwise have to be raised by income tax not only by increasing sales tax on some items that are already taxed but also by broadening the base of goods which are to be subject to sales tax. In fact, this year, the proportion of revenue raised by income tax will remain high even though there is an increase in sales tax. This year it is estimated that personal income tax will raise 55.8 per cent of the Commonwealth Budget's taxation revenues. Company tax will raise 12.6 per cent. It is worth looking at that figure because last year the proportion of the Budget raised from company tax was 13.9 per cent. The year before it was 15 per cent. Why the proportion of company tax has dropped is pretty clear. The rate of profitability of our companies in Australia has fallen because there has been an unbelievable wage push in Australia.
The proportion of revenue raised from customs, excise and sales tax will increase this year from 20.8 per cent to 22.2 per cent of total taxation revenues. The proportion of revenue arising from the crude oil levy will fall from 8.4 per cent last year to 7.4 per cent this year. So overall the

proportion of revenue arising from personal income tax will fall by less than one per cent. One reason is that the amounts raised from company tax and the crude oil levy will fall. The proportion of tax arising from the customs, excise and sales tax measures will increase and the proportion arising from personal income tax will remain about the same. However, I reiterate that, given economic recovery and increased company profitability, there is the prospect that revenue from company tax will rise and further reductions in personal tax could be a prospect.
I want to mention briefly two other points. If we continue to shift the burden from direct tax to indirect tax this will mean that those who buy goods for resale, which are subject to the tax, will have to find larger amounts of money to fund the purchases of those goods for the period they remain unsold. At the moment sales tax is payable 21 days after the month in which the goods were purchased by the person intending to resell them. Therefore, if we are to continue to go down the track of moving from direct to indirect taxation the Government will have to give consideration to the time which is given to pay these taxes. At the moment 21 days is allowed. It may well be that to ease the burden on the cash flow, particularly that of small businesses, that period ought to be reviewed to see if more time to pay should be given in the future.
The other matter I want to raise is the confusion which inevitably follows the announcements on Budget night of increases in sales tax. There was an example again this year of consumers going into shops and small businessmen not knowing whether to impose the new sales tax. There is a clear distinction between excise and sales tax. Some excises are imposed from the night of the Budget, but sales taxes are imposed on goods which are subsequently bought by the retailer. It is, therefore, not correct for the retailer to impose those sales taxes unless they are in respect of goods which are bought by the retailer following the Budget.
I believe the Commissioner of Taxation ought to make available an explanation of the difference between the excise and sales tax increases which inevitably occur in budgets. If such an explanation is readily available to small businessmen I think it would be of benefit to them and when changes occur after a Budget they would know whether they had to increase the prices of their goods. That will avoid the problem which arose in Adelaide this year when some businessmen put up the price because they did not know. Customers were aggrieved-reasonably and fairly so-by that. They took the matter up, I understand, with

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1185

the office of consumer affairs in South Australia. If the Commissioner of Taxation could make the above mentioned information available I think it would assist the small businessman. I conclude by wholeheartedly supporting these Bills as part of what I believe is a very welcome Government Budget.

I say quite emphatically that Australia does not raise a relatively small proportion of total taxation by way of indirect taxes. In fact, the situation in Australia is quite to the contrary.

That is the figure given by the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) in his contribution to this debate. The figure that has been given by the Treasurer before and which was repeated by the honourable member for Barker relates only to sales tax. Of course, it is very convenient for the Treasurer or for any other honourable member on the Government side simply to-

Both the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9) and the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill represent a continuing assault by this Government on the living costs and living standards of Australian families. The Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9) have the effect of increasing by 2+ percentage points all those increased rates which applied from last year anyway and the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill 1982 has the effect of bringing into the sales tax net a number of goods that had previously escaped it. There is no question that the Government is quite determined to continue this assault. It was thwarted last year in the miserable attempt that it made to inflict a 2t per cent sales tax on what were classified as the necessities of life, for example, kids' clothing and shoes, building materials for people's homes, and books and newspapers. Fortunately that was rejected by the Senate and the Government has not had the gall to reintroduce it-at least not on this occasion.

If that election takes place there will be a reversal in approach and the Government will introduce all these fiscal measures next year. The Government knows that, we know it and the Australian community knows it as well. Despite all the Government's rhetoric about adjusting the tax mix, the fact remains that indirect taxes are regressive, unjust, inequitable and create a much more severe burden on lower income earners. The approach that this Government has taken to this question throws into very serious doubt any suggestion that this Government might be humane or might have any compassionate feelings for those people in the community whom it continues to burden.
The repeated claim by both the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Treasurer in relation to indirect taxes is that there is some need to adjust the tax mix, to place more emphasis on indirect taxes, to provide scope for a reduction in personal income tax. In fact what is their real objective? I think this was indicated very clearly in a radio interview with the Prime Minister in Perth in December last year. He said:

sales tax intentions at that time. The following are not my words but are from a responsible, reputable and highly respected major provincial newspaper in Australia. The editorial stated:

The Sales Tax Amendment Bills are designed to impose a sales tax of 7.5 per cent in a range of goods that are now exempt from the sales tax under the First Schedule. Let us look at some of the things that will impact on households. There will be a 7t per cent sales tax on cleaning and polishing cloths, cleaning sponges, handkerchiefs, blankets, braid, ribbon and tape, household drapery and soft furnishings. Some of the goods taxed that will have an impact on industry are contained in the first list but as well as those are hand tools, engine cleaning cotton waste, and servicing and

construction equipment. Of course, last year the Government wanted to impose a 2-1: per cent tax on building materials but it was not allowed to get away with it. That particular provision is to give a net result to revenue this year of $44m and $105m over a full year.
The next provision is even more interesting and fascinating. Let us look at some of the provisions of the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9) for this year. First of all, the 5 per cent sales tax rate will be increased to 7.5 per cent. I do not think we should allow the claim to continue to be made that the increase is 2.5 per cent. In fact, it is not; it is an increase of 2.5 percentage points and the increase in the sales tax is 50 per cent. In two years the sales tax paid on those items included in the Third Schedule has increased by 200 per cent. The increase in sales tax on items in the Fourth and Fifth Schedules and those goods that were not covered by any Schedules has been from I7.5 per cent to 20 per cent. It was suggested again that that is an increase of 2.5 per cent. In fact, the increase is 14.3 per cent and it should be recognised as such. The sales tax levied on items in the Second Schedule has increased from 30 per cent to 32.5 per cent. Again, it has been suggested that the increase is 2.5 per cent. It is nothing of the kind; it is 8.3 per cent.
Let us look at the fascinating contrast between the 8.3 per cent increase in sales tax and the other increases. The percentage increase of 8.3 per cent will apply to things such as jewellery and furs, gold plated wear, perfumery and bath salts. I will now tell honourable members some of the things on which sales tax will increase by 14.3 per cent. They do not include the bath salts of the wealthy but the soap of the lower and middle income earners, kids' bicycles, soap powders, disinfectants, soft drinks, toilet paper and kids' toys. The percentage increase in sales tax on those items is 14.3 per cent but the percentage increase on the bath salts of the wealthy, the gold plated wear, the perfumery and the jewellery and furs of the wealthy is 8.3 per cent. The increase of 50 per cent goes on refrigerators, washing machines and a range of household goods. So the people who are struggling to get their homes together will pay an increase in sales tax of 50 per cent. What a miserable approach. Is there anyone on the Government side who can reasonably defend such a proposition? The increases will give a net result this

year of $370m and the rip-off over a full year will be another $490m.
Let me give the total increases in this Government's Budget from indirect taxes. The increase in the existing rate of 2.S percentage points, not 2.S per cent, will yield $490m in a full year. I will finish on this point: In 1975-76 the proportion of total taxation which was provided by indirect taxes was 28 per cent. That was at the end of the period of the last Labor Government. That Government had been able to reduce the proportion because it did not believe that indirect taxes should be raising the revenue. Now the proportion is back up to 30 per cent. There is no question that if the Australian people want equity built into the tax system and the way in which the Australian community is managed there is only one party in Australia that is determined to and capable of building equity into the system and that is the Australian Labor Party. The Australian people will have the opportunity to establish that change very shortly.
Mr MOUNTFORD (Banks) (S.O)-The Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos I to 9) which are before the House propose to provide for an overall increase in sales taxes of 2.S percentage points on all the items contained within the Schedules applicable to those Acts. The Bills also impose sales tax of 7.S per cent on a range of goods that are now exempt from any sales tax. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) has advised the House that the overall increases will provide revenue of $370m this financial year and $490m in a full year. Such increases were applicable from 18 August. We are told that the new sales tax of 7.S per cent applicable from I January 1983 will provide revenue of $44m this year and $lOSm in a full year.
The total revenue from sales tax in 1982-83 is estimated by the Treasury to be $3,629m, an increase of 27.1 per cent over last year. When one considers that the increases last year were 3S.8 per cent over those collected in 1980-81, the amount

of revenue now being collected by the Government shows up starkly as being a particularly heavy burden on the Australian community. The total revenue increase in sales tax since 1980-81 has been $1,S27m or 72.6 per cent. In cold hard terms the increases in sales taxes over the last two years mean that a family of four is paying an additional $7 per week on the acquisition of goods and services. If the excise increases on fuel are included the rise in indirect taxes over the two year period amounts to $8.40 per week.
In February this year, when debating the Government's Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos lA to 9A) 1981 [No.2], I predicted that by next year the Government would be extending the sales tax provisions to incorporate the taxing of food and services in accordance with the suggestions made by the Treasury in its 1974 Taxation Paper No. S dealing with Commonwealth taxation of goods and services. My prediction, which was vehemently denied by Government members at that time, although not yet completely fulfilled, has been partially vindicated. The new sales tax of 7.S per cent to be applied to a range of goods and services bites further into the area which has never been subject to taxes before. On 17 September 1981 I had incorporated in Hansard appendices D and E of Treasury Taxation Paper No. S of 1974. I seek leave again to have those appendices incorporated in Hansard because they are relevant to the present legislation.

Mr MOUNTFORD-I thank the House. From an examination of these tables it is easy to see how the Government is gradually taxing by stealth those goods and services which were previously exempt. In the 1981 Budget, tax was applied to many of the goods shown in appendix D. Those items which escaped the tax in 1981 have now in the main been caught by the present legislation. The major item still not gripped for sales tax is foodstuffs. Turning now to appendix E, we see that for the first time the Government is going into the services area to collect sales tax. It is hitting the repairs section which, incidentally, will yield the second highest return after travel costs on which sales taxes will now be levied. I would think that after the Government's reversal on previous promises made on the imposition of indirect taxes I am on fairly safe ground still by saying that within 12 months this Government intends imposing sales tax on foodstuffs and the remainder of the goods and services outlined in appendices D and E. Government members can deny those remarks all they wish but the actions of this Government to date in this area have proved otherwise.
The Treasurer in his Budget Speech to the House this year and last year said that the Government is seeking to move gradually away from a reliance on personal tax as a source of revenue to a widening of the indirect tax base. 1 should like to outline some of the goods and services which will now be subject to the imposition of sales tax for the first time and others which will be subject to the overall increase of 2.5 percentage points. My reason for outlining some of the items is to show how the disposable incomes of the Australian community will be affected under these measures. The Treasurer, in his Budget Speech, dismissed the increases in two and a half lines when he said:
This broadening of the sales tax base is to include goods such as hand tools, construction equipment and manchester. It does not include what some have regarded as the 'necessities of life'.
The legislation now under debate provides for a 7.5 per cent sales tax to be imposed on goods and services which until now had not been subject to any tax. I have not time in this debate to go through all the matters, but if we look through the schedules, excluding the items now to be gripped by this tax, we see quite a number of household items listed: Household drapery and soft furnishings, namely, bedsheets, bedspreads, curtains and textile cords, loose furniture covers, pillowcases, quilts, tablecloths, table covers, table mats, table

Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9)

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1189

napkins, table runners and doilies, tea-towels, towels, face cloths and face washers, blankets, travelling rugs and handkerchiefs made of cloth. One could go on and on, but time does not permit. Goods now being taxed at 32.5 per cent compared with 30 per cent last year include radio and television receivers, record players, cameras, video recorders, watches, jewellery, et cetera. Tax on these goods will raise $565m this financial year, an increase of 18 per cent over last year. As my colleague the honourable member for Hunter (Mr Robert Brown) pointed out a few minutes ago, these items are bought by people who can well afford them and who are not being subject to the same percentage increases as those on the lower scale.
Goods now being taxed at 20 per cent compared with 17.5 per cent last year include motor cars and commercial vehicles, boats, confectionery, stationery, computers, soft drinks, et cetera. Tax on these goods will raise $2,764m this year, an increase of 24.6 per cent over last year. Goods now subject to a tax of 7.5 per cent compared with 5 per cent last year include household furniture, appliances such as refrigerators, washing machines and other household goods. Tax on these goods will raise $300m this year, an increase
of 92.3 per cent over last year. So we can see where the burden is falling. As I mentioned previously, the total sales tax collections this year of $3,629m are an increase of 27.1 per cent over last year and represent an extra payout of $3 per week for the average family man with a wife and two children. The present increases in sales tax are completely reprehensible as they hit hardest those on lower incomes. The statement by the Treasurer that the goods being taxed do not include necessities of life is not a feasible one. Would anyone really suggest that household furniture, refrigerators, stoves, mattresses, blankets, bedsheets, towels and tablecloths, to name a few items, are not necessities of life? If the Treasurer does not consider such items as necessities of life, he and other Government members are suggesting a very low standard of living for the Australian community. Of course these and many other goods being taxed are necessities of life and as

such should be free of any tax. Irrespective of a family's income, these goods are a necessity in the home. To back up my statement that indirect taxes fall heaviest on those on lower incomes, I refer the House to a number of studies carried out in this area. The first study I refer to was carried out by the Committee for Economic Development of Australia and is titled 'The Reform of the Commonwealth Government Sales Tax' and is dated August 1982. On page 10 of that report, under the heading 'Equity', it states:
In assessing the equity of a tax it is useful to distinguish between horizontal and vertical equity. To satisfy horizontal equity, a tax should impose the same burdens on individuals having an equal capacity to pay tax. Vertical equity requires that individuals with unequal capacity to pay be treated differently and in accordance with that inequality.
Vertical equity considerations suggest that a tax should be proportional or progressive with respect to income. Hence there is concern about the regressive nature of a broad-based indirect tax. Although essentially proportional in terms of consumption expenditure, the tax is regressive as related to income. This is because the proportion of income spent on consumption goods declines with movement up the income scale. The question then' arises as to whether any regressivity introduced by the proposed changes should be compensated by income tax or social welfare adjustment.
It is obvious that the tax cuts afforded the community offer only an indexation catch-up and in no way compensate for the massive increases in indirect taxes. The next study I refer to is the 1975 report of the Taxation Review Committee, under the chairmanship of Mr Justice Asprey. It showed quite clearly that taxation is very sharply regressive at the lower end of the household income scale, nearly proportional in a wide middle band, and progressive only at higher levels. The reason given was that indirect taxes are chiefly responsible for this occurrence. The Committee prepared a table showing taxes as a proportion of income for the year 1966-67 and, although the income levels are now largely outdated, I seek leave to have this table incorporated in Hansard because I believe it shows how indirect taxes fall heaviest on lower income earners.
Leave granted.

Page 2, Schedule, after clause I insert the following clause:
"IA. Item 107A in the First Schedule is amended by omitting from paragraph (I) (c) 'item 107' and substituting 'item 6 in the Third Schedule'. ".
This amendment to the Bill is necessary as a result of motion picture films becoming taxable. Item 107 in the First Schedule to the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act which exempts motion picture films is to be omitted and will be inserted in the same form as item 6 in the Third Schedule. Goods covered by the Third Schedule are to be taxable at 7.5 per cent.
Item 107A, First Schedule, provides exemption for certain goods for use for business or industrial purposes in the production of motion picture films. It is to remain in the First Schedule but paragraph 1 (c) of that item refers to apparatus

Sales Tax Amendment Bill

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1191

and materials for use in developing, editing or otherwise processing goods to which sub-item (2) of item 107 applies. With the omission of item 107 from the First Schedule the reference in paragraph I (c) of item 107A to item 107 (2) is of no effect.
To ensure that the exemption provided in paragraph I (c) of item 107Aoperates as intended, it is necessary to alter the reference in that item to refer to sub-item (2) of item 6 in the Third Schedule. This consequential drafting amendment was overlooked in drafting the original Bill. The amendment to the Bill will correct the position.
Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (5.l7)-This amendment is of a purely technical nature and as such the Opposition does not oppose it although in listening to the speech of the Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Mr Hodgman) it was hard to discern precisely what it is about. The package of sales tax Bills that we have dealt with and and the sales tax amendment that we are dealing with now indicate in the general sense the Government's increasing reliance on the use of sales taxes as and indirect taxes as a way of raising revenue. We have seen in this Budget that the Government is to raise approximately $1,500m extra in revenue from indirect taxes. We have seen the burden of taxation in this country moved from those people who can afford it to those who cannot afford it. Indirect taxes affect everybody equally. They affect people on high salaries at the same rate as they affect people on low salaries, people who have no income and pensioners.
As the honourable member for Banks (Mr Mountford) said earlier, the present Government's increases in sales taxes and indirect taxes will cost a family of four in Australia approximately $7 a week extra to live. That to my mind represents a very unfair shift of the burden of taxation in this country. In fact in this Budget we have seen a quite significant increase in other forms of tax for ordinary income earners. Pay-asyou-earn taxes as a proportion of total tax collections have increased from 43.5 per cent in 1981-82 to 45.4 per cent in 1981-82. In the same period we have seen company taxes decline from 14.2 per cent of total tax collections to 11.9 per cent. When we couple that increase in net PAYE taxation with the sales taxes and indirect taxes that have been levied by this Government it is quite plain that the Government is embarking on a deliberate approach of making low income earners and ordinary wage earners pay the cost of its economic program. We have seen the Government put forward the somewhat phoney Bicentennial Road Development Program and a levy

on petrol to ensure that that Program will be paid for. Once again, that will flow through to the cost of goods and services for ordinary Australians. That is the sort of revenue that should be raised by methods other than a tax on petrol.
Whilst the Labor Government when it was in office reduced the reliance on indirect taxation the coalition presently in office has reversed that trend and, in line with the stated preferences of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), has moved towards a heavier burden of indirect taxes, due mainly to the rapid growth of taxes such as the oil levy, which I have just mentioned, and sales taxes. The Government has attacked even such areas as sport. It is interesting to note that the Government's increase of 2+ percentage points in the sales tax on sporting goods, from IS per cent to 17+ per cent, will provide it with a revenue in the next financial year of approximately $90m. Of course, one has to contrast that with the $3.7m that the Government provides to assist sporting bodies. On the one hand the Government takes $90m away from people through the sales tax on sporting goods and on the other hand it gives a paltry $3.7m in grants to sporting organisations.
We have also seen in the perversion of the taxation system under this Government a terrible crime perpetrated on pensioners. The Government seems to have missed the fact that many pensioners are still paying mortgages. While one can applaud the Government's proposal to provide some relief to people who are paying off their homes by giving a tax rebate, one can only look in dismay at the fact that the rebate does not apply to pensioners or other people on low incomes who do not pay tax because unfortunately they do not earn a taxable income. I think when these things are taken into account the moving of the tax system to rely on these broad-based consumeroriented taxes rather than on taxes on dividends and on high salaries is to be deplored. I feel that the Government should look again at this method of raising revenue. I think it should look again at the proposals in this Budget which ensure that those people who are on low incomes or very moderate incomes are penalised once again. The Budget's tax cuts and other tax measures are great for those people who have money. For those who do not have much money they are dismal.
The proposals with regard to sales taxes and other such indirect taxes that we have been discussing today in this Parliament place more and more pressure on low income families in Australia who are going to the wall every day. To raise $1,500m through the type of tax proposed in this

Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (5.26)-1 never cease to be amazed at the double talk that one hears in this Budget debate from one Opposition speaker after another. The Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) seeks to convince the Australian people that he and the Australian Labor Party alone have the economic recipe that will solve all our economic problems, reduce inflation, restore profits, create jobs, inspire confidence and get our economy back on the right road. But how could one forget Labor's disastrous economic management when it was in government? In 1972, when the Labor Party was elected to office, the inflation rate was 4.5 per cent. Three years later it had risen to 17 per cent. The unemployment level in this country was 2.6 per cent when it was elected and three years later it had nearly doubled.
Opposition members interjecting-
Mr HUNT-Honourable members opposite can play the violin as much as they like but they will not drown out the memories of the Australian people of the mess that they made of this country

while they were in government. The government deficit rose--
Mr Kerin-It is seven years ago.
Mr HUNT-Memories are not as short as that. Seven years will not separate the Australian Labor Party from the memories of the Australian people. When the Labor Party took office the Budget deficit rose from 0.4 per cent to 4.9 per cent of the gross domestic product. In three short years the whole nation was plunged into an economic crisis of unprecedented proportions. In three years we endured three Treasurers who lurched from one desperate policy to another, from printing money to Khemlani loans. The present Leader of the Opposition presented to this Parliament an irresponsible Budget which led to a Budget deficit representing 4.9 per cent of gross domestic product. The present Budget has a deficit of only one per cent of the GDP-one of the lowest deficits in the western world.
The Australian Labor Party in government succeeded in plunging this country into an economic crisis. The Australian Labor Party in government presided over the worst years of industrial disputation on record. In 1974 we witnessed 6.3 million days lost and $128m lost in wages. The Australian Labor Party in government succeeded in destroying our reputation as an overseas trading partner and a nation that could be relied upon for safe and stable investment. The Australian Labor Party in government embarked upon an irresponsible spending spree which resulted in a huge Budget deficit year after year, inflation and rising unemployment. Yet the Australian Labor Party today seeks to convince this Parliament and, indeed, the electorate that it has the economic remedies, the answers to the current international economic problems and the internal economic difficulties.
1 contend that the Labor Party neither has the answers to solve the current economic problems nor the capacity or the will to do so. Nothing demonstrated this more to this House and to this nation than the Leader of the Opposition's speech in this debate and the so-called agreement between the Australian Labor Party and the Australian Council of Trade Unions on prices and incomes. There is no agreement with the individual trade unions over which the ACTU has no real control. What does it mean however? It means that a Labor Government would support full wage indexation and higher welfare spending in the hope that the unions will agree to wage moderation. It means that hundreds of thousands of people who are now employed could well lose their jobs if full indexation is restored. It means also increased government spending, higher

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1193

deficits, increased inflation and worsening economic circumstances in one of the worst international recessions this century.
There are no easy answers or easy options available to any government in Australia. There are no easy options available to the Australian people. The sooner the Opposition and the trade union movement recognise the hard truth the better the long term prospects for all Australians. Although in 1981-82 our real growth was 3 per centindeed it has been one-and-a-half times the average of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development countries since 1978 and we had one of the highest increases in private capital investment for 30 years-the truth of the matter is that our economy is now in recession. Our difficulties need to be viewed against the background of the worst world recession since the 1930s, a fall or stagnation in six of the major world economies in the March quarter-nobody can deny that as much as honourable members on the other side of the House might weep-the worst slump in world trade for over two decades, huge unemployed popUlations in the industrialised nations, a severe slump in the world steel industry, serious problems amongst many major overseas banking corporations--

Mr HUNT-It is quite clear that honourable members opposite cannot take the truth. There are serious problems amongst many major overseas banking corporations. There is a slump in world commodity prices and high interest rates in most overseas countries. On the domestic front our problems will be exacerbated by one of the worst droughts in eastern Australia this century, severe balance of trade problems and high interest rates. These problems are the most serious in some of the rural industries which are essential to the economy and to foreign exchange earnings. I represent one of the worst drought affected regions in Australia. On behalf of my constituents I welcome the initiatives taken by the Commonwealth Government to alleviate some of the burdens that the farmers, graziers and rural communities are carrying at the present time. The subsidy on interest rates above 12 per cent and the 50 per cent subsidy on fodder and cartage will give the farmers some heart and hope. If an effort had not been made by the Commonwealth Government to encourage the feeding of breeding stock I have no doubt that there would have been enormous stock losses.
There are difficulties ahead. There is no way in the current world economic recession that any government can protect every person, every employer and every industry from the full effects of the recession. However, a government can do its best to lessen the impact of that recession. I believe that this Budget and the recently announced drought relief measures go a long way toward helping to lessen the impact and to stimulate economic activity to the maximum extent possible.
The overall Budget strategy will provide assistance to those in need, incentive for investment, growth in the construction industry and job opportunities. All forms of Commonwealth welfare allowances have been increased. The average family with two dependent children and a home mortgage will be better off by $17 a week. Incentives to investment will be given by tax rebates on housing interest payments; substantial outlays to industry, including taxation incentives and depreciation allowances costing $700m; rebates on the first $1,000 of company dividends; increased retention allowances for small businesses; and tax incentives for employees to buy shares in the companies for which they work. Assistance to rural industry will be increased by over 40 per cent to more than $300m. There will be growth in the construction industry, and this will certainly be assisted by the home ownership and housing incentive policies already announced. There will also be increased funding of welfare housing,

1194 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

increased funding of aged and handicapped persons' accommodation, substantial increases of 26 per cent in funds for major Commonwealth public works programs, and increased assistance to the States for public works.
The introduction of the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program will add an estimated $2,500m to present road funds over the next six years. The Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program will transform Australia's road network. It is the most significant roads program ever initiated in Australia. I hope the Opposition supports it to the hilt. It will benefit all Australians and will be particularly welcomed by decentralised and tourist industries, city businesses, transport operators, private motorists and indeed the primary and rural sector. The estimated $2.5 billion will substantially boost the level of Commonwealth financial assistance previously allocated to the States and the Northern Territory for roads. Expenditure this financial year is expected to be $150m. The total road funding by the Commonwealth in 1982-83 under the Bicentennial Road Development Program and the current ongoing program to the States and the Northern Territory will amount to $914m. This is an increase of 33 per cent, or $229m, when compared with the $685m allocated last financial year.
In 1983-84 when the ABRD program is fully underway Commonwealth expenditure for both road programs will be an estimated $1,208m, an increase of $523m, or 76 per cent, over 1981-82. Under the ABRD program the States and the Northern Territory will be required at least to maintain their own expenditure on roads in real terms. This is to ensure that the program funds are fully devoted to additional efforts on our roads. The major objectives of the program are to bring the national highway system to acceptable standards by 1988 so that it provides for the safe, reliable and efficient carriage of traffic between Australia's main centres; to accelerate the construction of current developmental road projects, including roads of national tourist importance; to assist the development of major urban and rural arterial roads and, when desired by the States, to provide assistance for approved urban public transport projects; and to enable local government authorities to upgrade their local road systems by 1988.
Motorists will be able to drive between all mainland State and Territory capital cities on completely sealed and virtually flood-free surfaces by the bicentennial year. Large parts of our heavily trafficked roads will be provided with divided carriageways and by-passes of major towns.

Substantial lengths of existing two-lane sealed roads will be reconstructed and widened, including narrow or flood-prone bridges. About 60 per cent of the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program funds will be allocated to the States and to the Northern Territory and their local government authorities for rural and urban arterial roads and local roads. There will, of course, be significant benefits to all road users: Savings in vehicle operating costs and travel time will be realised. Delays due to flooding which have been so common in North Queensland along the Bruce Highway and dust hazards which have been so notable on the Landsborough Highway in Queensland will be reduced. Most importantly, road safety will be enhanced. Probably one of the most important features of the program is that it will create job opportunities throughout Australia, particularly in the regional areas. It is anticipated that the program will directly create more than 8,000 new jobs in the road construction industry. All in all we expect that a total of at least some 20,000 jobs in direct and indirect employment will be created. A large proportion of these jobs, as I said earlier, will be in regional areas where jobs are so badly needed at present.
The Government is determined to ensure that the taxpayers get full value for the charges they are being asked to pay to finance this program. The revenues from this year's one cent per litre increase in the price of petrol and two cents per litre increase thereinafter will be earmarked and paid into a trust fund for expenditure on this program until 31 December 1988. The funds will not be spent for any other purpose. They will not be syphoned off into other expenditure areas. Apart from a substantial increase in capital funding for roads, the transport and construction portfolio expenditure provides for a substantial increase in capital works generally. All told, some $2,000m will be outlayed during 1982-83. That is an increase of 18.4 per cent, or $307.4m, when compared with 1981-82. There will be a 26 per cent increase in capital works this financial year. This expenditure of $368.4m will provide a significant boost to the construction industry. Almost all projects will be carried out under contract. About 50 per cent of the design work is done by private consultants at present. The construction and associated service industries will therefore share substantially in the increased activity. Repairs and maintenance of civil and defence facilities will increase by 14 per cent to $172 million, and the projects of course will become part of the national asset.
Apart from the recent decision to revitalise the Australian shipping industry on a commercial

Appropriation Bill (No. 1) 1982-83

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1195

basis, several other initiatives are under way to enhance the capacity of the surface transport area to service efficiently the transport needs of Australians. In this session of Parliament I will be introducing legislation to improve the efficiency of the Australian National Railways Commission. That legislation will enable the Australian National Railways to adopt a more commercial approach, subject to any specific ministerial direction in respect of Public Service obligations. A deficit of $71.5m has been provided for this year but the aim is to achieve financial break-even by 1988.
Mr Deputy Speaker, the Government, in association with the States, provides support for the Australian Railways Research and Development Organisation. As I said earlier, the Budget provides for significant development of the Australian transport system which, together with the increased capital works expenditure, will provide a significant boost to employment, particularly in the construction industry. I am determined to obtain maximum value from the substantial outlays that have been made by the Government in this area. Australia's prosperity is vitally linked to our trading performance, which in turn depends on an efficient transport system and capital investment. During the coming year the Australian Railways Research and Development Organisation will be holding a series of workshops and a seminar on rail policy issues, seeking to reach agreement between the States to reduce railways deficits. Also, in association with the States and the Northern Territory, a joint working party has been established under the auspices of the Australian Transport Advisory Council to examine the development of an Australian transport strategy which recognises the respective roles of all three tiers of government. This examination was proposed by the Transport Industries Advisory Council. It will not be an introspective in-house examination. Anybody who has a particular view about our transport system-I mean users and operators alike-will have an opportunity to put that view forward.
This Budget will give the economy the degree of help that is required at present. It will provide job opportunities particularly in the construction area. The Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program will undoubtedly not only provide job opportunities but also will ultimately lay the foundations for a really first class national road network. I can see why the honourable member for Hindmarsh (Mr Scott) is smiling. He has a lot to smile about because he loves driving on good roads.
