Mr CAMPBELL (Kalgoorlie) (5.46)- Beneath the tinsel and show of this year's Budget lies an admission of this Government's bankruptcy. The Treasurer (Mr Howard), in his speech, admitted:
. . . It is difficult to envisage any real growth in domestic non-farm product in 1982-83 . . .
Farm production will decline; in 1982-83 business fixed investment could well decline; real private dwelling investment is still forecast to decline faster in 1982-83 than it did in the previous year; there is unlikely to be any real growth in employment in 1982-83. So with the labour force continuing to grow unemployment therefore seems likely to increase; and the outlook for the balance of payments will reflect the developing outlook both at home and overseas. In other words, our position will be gloomy. Not since the Menzies Government in 1961 has the Australian economy failed to grow for a whole year. Not within parliamentary memory has a government forecast no growth. This is a statement of political bankruptcy and the Government must take much of the blame for this state of affairs.
This Budget has been called a family Budget. Certainly concessions have been made to the family by means of increased family allowances and income tax relief. However, when one takes a close look at the Budget it becomes apparent that the Treasurer has taken more by means of sales tax, Customs and excise levies, bank duties, et cetera than he has afforded in income tax relief and sundry handouts. This Budget is cosmetic.
Income tax is, of course, the key revenue component in the Budget. This year some $18,973m will be levied from wage and salary earners. That is 45.4 per cent of the estimated Budget receipts. Another $4,373m will come from the selfemployed thus providing a further 10 per cent of estimated receipts. The Government's income tax package is a mixed bag. The Government is to be commended on the steps it has taken-limited, awkward and hesitant though they may be-in tackling the problem of tax avoidance. This move is long overdue. The Government has consistently refused to use its legislative powers to tackle tax avoiders. Treasurer Howard was briefed in 1978 about bottom-of-the-harbour schemes. However it took him two and a half years to introduce the Crimes (Taxation Offences) Act. I fear that this Government will not achieve even the modest target it has set itself in this regard, given the fierce opposition from within the Liberal Party's own

ranks especially from my State of Western Australia. In any case, how can the Government know how much the Australian Taxation Office will be able to claw back when the Treasurer will not state how far back he will go to collect the money? This is a question which the community should be asking. After all, the money that the community will gain from tax avoiders is money lost to Liberal Party coffers. The Government has to temper its community responsibilities because of its party liabilities.

Mr CAMPBELL-Thank you, Mr Deputy Speaker. I did think that my remarks were modest. It is unfortunate that the Government has not seen fit to provide any substantial relief for the far-flung residents of my electorate. Last year, for the first time in a decade, the zone allowance rebate was adjusted. Now a taxpayer who lives in Zone A will get a base amount of $216 plus 50 per cent of the dependant's rebate. A taxpayer who lives in Zone B will get a base allowance of $36 plus 20 per cent of the dependant's rebate. Last

year a special category was created which allowed a $750 rebate plus a Zone A or Zone B dependant rebate, depending on locality, for any taxpayer who resides more than 250 kilometres from a population centre of 250,000 people or more. The creation of this special isolated zone was a step in the right direction, although it created severe anomalies in many places in my electorate. For example Leanora and Yalgoo are most unfortunate as they miss out by only a few kilometres.
It is unfortunate also that the Government again appears set on ignoring the needs of remote area residents for another decade. The current allowance is mere tokenism. At its existing level it cannot have any effect on decentralisation or provide any significant compensation for the disadvantage of living in remote areas. The Government must move now to increase tax allowances.
Once again, the Government has gone for an increase in sales tax. In 1982-83 total sales tax payments are expected to amount to $3,629m compared with $2,854m last year and $2,102m the year before that. In two years the share of the Budget revenue derived from sales tax has risen from 6.5 per cent to 8.7 per cent. I am opposed to this trend. I have said in this House before and I repeat it again now that all sales taxes are iniquitous; sales tax is indiscriminate. Everyone has to pay sales tax regardless of his ability to pay. This form of taxation hits poor and country people more than anyone else. Sales tax particularly discriminates against country people as it is levied on a percentage of the price of goods that pass from the wholesaler to the retailer and there is a freight component as well as the unit cost. As a result of sales tax increases, residents throughout my electorate are footing more of the overall tax bill this year.
The Fraser Government appears to subscribe to President Reagan's dictum that graduated income tax was invented by Karl Marx. We certainly appear to be heading down the road to an American situation of far greater reliance on indirect taxation. This is a trend that I feel must be reversed. As a result of this year's Budget an extra $595m will be levied from consumers in increased sales taxes. Local government suffers as it is guaranteed only a percentage of income tax revenue. I believe that this Liberal Government is determined to undermine the limited financial independence afforded to this most important area by previous Labor governments.
Last year in my speech on the Budget. I warned that once an item appeared in the schedule and became taxable the amount of tax levied would start to speed up. We now find that items such as

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1197

radios, televisions, stereos, cameras and jewellery will be levied at 32.5 per cent. Furniture, air conditioners, refrigerators and most electrical goods will be subject to a 17.5 per cent sales tax. Motor vehicles, spirits, soft drinks, toys and a whole host of other items will now be levied at 20 per cent. A number of items that were previously exempt will be subject to a 7.5 per cent duty. These include maps, hand tools, soft furnishings and servicing, repair and construction equipment. The tax on servicing, repairing and reconditioning equipment for motor vehicles, aeroplanes, ships and railway rolling stock will inevitably force up transport costs within my electorate and indeed throughout the whole nation.
Pet food is now subject to a 20 per cent duty. This must seriously affect the commercial exploitation of kangaroos. It is just another example of this Government's concern for country jobs. This issue was taken up by a Liberal Party member for Lower North in the Western Australian Upper House who was lamenting the Government's decision to tax the industry so savagely.
This year, as a result of the Budget, we find that a special surcharge on motor spirit and distillate is to be introduced. This must raise between $300m and $400m in a full year when the 2c a litre surcharge is reached. The only redeeming feature of the surcharge is that it will be set aside in a special trust account for road works. My electors will want to see some appreciable road works before they cop this. I have always held the opinion that sufficient funds are derived from existing crude oil and liquefied petroleum gas excise duties to cover an impressive road construction program. I note that this year the oil levy is up 3.9 per cent and will bring $3,287m into Government coffers. I cannot support this double tax on the motorist just simply in the name of road works. A portion of the existing crude oil levy should be set aside for road works.
The Budget envisages lifting the exemption from excise duty for off-road diesel fuel. This is designed to yield $176m in a full year. This new duty will adversely affect agriculture and the fishing and mining industries in my electorate. It must lead to higher transport and power charges. Bricks and road building materials will cost more, so will fuel for cranes, tractors, bulldozers, excavation equipment, on-site power generators, and other diesel-fired machinery. Once again, the country suffers more than the city. The Government is intent on gaining an extra $12.5m from increased air navigation charges. This will inevitably make it more expensive to travel within Western Australia and interstate. Air fares are already very high. It currently costs $90 to fly

from Kalgoorlie to Perth. It costs $174.20 to fly from Port Hedland to Perth and $291.50 from Kununurra to Perth. Since I got those figures a few days ago there has been a substantial increase in fares of the order of 7 per cent. The Government should be moving to check air fare price rises, not fuelling the fires. The very least it can do is to allow Trans Australia Airlines to compete on some of the lucrative interstate routes.
I now turn my attention to some of the Budget outlays. Despite their much heralded announcement, many Budget allocations are paltry. Under Prime Minister Fraser unemployment is a growth industry. How does he treat the unemployed? Unemployment and sickness benefits for young people under 18 years of age will rise by only $4 a week from $36 to $40-the first increase since 1978-and by only $6.30 from $58.10 to $64.40 for people over 18 years of age. The Government will no doubt expect these beneficiaries to live gratefully on the smell of an oily rag for another four years. Once again, pensioners received a pittance, with a $3.10 a week increase for single pensioners and a $5.20 a week increase for couples. No attempt has been made to index pensions realistically. It is commendable that the limit on permissible pensioner earnings has been increased, albeit for the first time in 10 years. I just wonder how many pensioners will be able to earn the extra income, given the current state of the economy. The easing of the income test requirement for the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme and Secondary Allowances Scheme is welcome. This will not, however, significantly ease the burden for parents in the north of Western Australia, where wages and costs tend to be high. I welcome the increase in funds for assistance programs for young unemployed. I regret that no significant plan for job expansion is contained in this Budget.
In the time. I have left I wish to say a little about the Liberal Party philosophy which underlies this Budget. Insofar as any economic theory has controlled the Fraser Government's economic policy, it has been based on these nostrums: Firstly, inflation is caused by deficits; secondly, if deficits fall inflation falls; thirdly, higher economic growth and a rising inflation rate are mutually exclusive; fourthly, a rising inflation rate makes people save a greater proportion of their income and therefore suppresses aggregate demand and growth; and fifthly, interest rate increases are caused by public sector borrowing demand and interest rates will fall if public sector borrowing demand falls. The great bulk of Australian statistical evidence contradicts all these beliefs. This

In the last five years there has been a doubling of the number of New Zealanders coming to settle in Australia. I will refer to another couple of points as they relate to Budget outlays. I mentioned the development work at Brisbane International Airport and the tremendous advances that could happen there. I was disturbed in recent days to see that the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works had recommended that the extensions to the new Brisbane International Airport should not be completed until 1990. I congratulate the Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife) for overriding that decision and deciding to continue with the works so that the project will be completed late in 1986 or early 1987, virtually on schedule. There has been some tremendous development there.
I took out some statistics on passenger arrivals and departures at Brisbane Airport during the last financial year. Last year 230,785 people arrived at the Brisbane Airport terminal. That was an increase of 17.25 per cent. Departures were up to 223,904 people, an increase of 20.39 per cent. Interest in Brisbane as an international destination is obvious. There were four new international carriers servicing that airport in the first six months of this year and another three carriers are giving indications that they will start services into Brisbane either later this year or early next year.

Obviously this raises the question of what is happening in the aviation market. Admittedly Queensland is becoming one of the major tourist destinations of the South Pacific but one cannot help but be perturbed at the continuing battle raging between the Commonwealth and New South Wales governments as to the future of Sydney International Airport. One would think there would be some very great argument in saying to the New South Wales Government that if it is not interested in coming to some agreement on exactly what should happen at the Sydney Airport complex we should look at making Brisbane the No.2 airport in Australia or indeed, if need be, even the No.1 airport. With the completion of that particular project it will have a lot of facilities going for it that, frankly, are not available in Sydney. One can look at the potential of what can be derived from a curfew free airport, which the new airport will be. One can also look at the prospect of putting into operation feeder services out of the Brisbane area to other parts of Australia. Certainly there is a lot of merit in this for the international operators. They can see some very real savings in the use of the new Brisbane International Airport complex.
Once again I would like to extend my thanks, and indeed the thanks of the people of my electorate, for that decision to continue with work on the new Brisbane International Airport and to go ahead on schedule. There is no doubt that aircraft noise has been a tremendous problem in the bayside suburbs of Wynnum, Manly and Lota and the areas of Coorparoo and Camp Hill in my electorate. It is good to know that the Government has acknowledged some of these difficulties and is prepared to go ahead and provide that sort of relief to the people of my electorate by continuing to complete that airport project.
Another area of the Budget which has raised quite a deal of comment in Queensland is foreign aid. Unfortunately, some rather prominent groups and personalities in Queensland say that perhaps Australia is spending too much on aid and we should be looking after our own first rather than developing some of the international aid projects. Whilst not coming into that argument, I was particularly perturbed at outbursts in the Press in Brisbane about the $10m that is being promised to the people who have been suffering in the Lebanon as a result of the war that has been continuing there since the early 1970s. In fact, the $10m that has been allocated I believe is a generous donation which will be used for humanitarian purposes. One can only support the Government in giving that aid and urge the

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

people of Australia to give as much as they possibly can to help relieve the suffering that is happening at the moment in the Lebanon.
One would hope that following the withdrawal of the Palestine Liberation Organisation and the election of a new President, Mr Beshir Gemayel, who takes up his new position in the next few weeks, at last the people of that strife torn country will be able to receive some sort of peace. They have not been able to enjoy any sort of existence there for the last 12 years. It was a war in which the Lebanese themselves were not involved. It was a war which a number of Arab factions from that part of the world decided that they would fight on Lebanese territory. It was amazing to know that in fact the PLO seemed to be fighting a number of factions, the Iraquis and the Iranians were fighting their own particular war and the PLO and the Kuwaitis were having exchanges from time to time with the people of Beirut, especially West Beirut, being virtually caught in the cross-fire. Nobody is denying that the new President of the Lebanon, Beshir Gemayel, has a very hard task in trying to restore some semblance of order to that strife torn land. We can only wish him the best and hope that in some way there can be a sense of national reconciliation in that country.
Lebanon is one of the oldest civilizations in the world. It is a country that has recognised human rights for hundreds of years. It is a country in which people of different religions and persuasions have managed to live side by side in peace and harmony for the last 400 years. It was sad to see the dominance of human rights being torn apart by a war that was not of the making of the Lebanese people. We can only shudder in horror at the thought that elements in the Australian society would deny any aid we can give to that strife torn country. Of course, the Government has already acknowledged that donations to those campaigns will be tax deductible.

There was also a recognition that very great consideration must be given to the

aged and the underprivileged in our society, and that was given. To a great extent the Budget was a humane Budget. It is one that should be welcomed by all the Australian community. It showed real compassion, while in very hard circumstances it gave room for people to make their own way in Australia once again and for Australia to take advantage of the world situation once it comes out of the recession that is causing havoc world wide.

Mr WEST (Cunningham) (8.25)~ This
Government and its Cabinet are undoubtedly the most incompetent, arrogant, divisive and hypocritical in Australia's history. The Government's incompetence is matched only by its hypocrisy and arrogance. It has introduced a no-hope Budget masquerading as a welfare Budget. But its welfare aspects are long overdue. In most cases the welfare elements are inadequate. They involve simply the indexation of benefits, pension thresholds and tax scales that should have been introduced long ago. The question is: When is a tax cut not a tax cut? The answer is, firstly, when it has elements of simple application of the consumer price index for 1981-82 to the existing tax scales and, secondly, when the total of increases in sales tax and excise duty more than equals the cut of 2c in the dollar in the basic tax scale. All that has happened is that the 10.4 per cent CPI rise for 1981-82 has been applied to the first two tax scales, which lifted the threshold of the first scale by $400 to $4,595 and that of the second scale by $1,606 to $19,500. So that is only a simple application of the 10.4 per cent consumer price index rise for 1981-82. The cut of 2c in the dollar in the basic tax rate is the only real tax cut in the Budget but that is more than offset by increases in sales tax and excise duty. The cut of 2c in the dollar on the lower tax scale means a loss to revenue of $706m this financial year and $1,066m in a full year. That is almost precisely equal to the increase in income resulting from increases in sales tax and excise duty, which amount to $786m in this financial year and over $1 billion in a full year.
The increase of 50 per cent to the income test threshold for age pensions is welcome but it is long overdue, simply being a recognition that no adjustment, at all had taken place since 1972. There is still no real increase, apart from the CPI adjustment, to base pensions. The unemployment benefit for single people is still well below the poverty line. However, behind the belated concessions that I have mentioned, if one reads the fine print of the Budget one will find a picture of unmitigated gloom. For example, the ~ Budget

Papers say that we can expect no real growth in non-farm output for 1982-83. Unemployment over the last financial year had risen by 100,000 to 460,500 as at 30 June last. In the last two years interest rates have gone up 4 per cent to the point where the Commonwealth Savings Bank rate on home loans is now a record 13.5 per cent, the building societies' rate is 15 per cent plus and the two-year treasury bond rate is 17.25 per cent. The balance of payments position is a complete trauma and disaster; the current account is $9 billion in deficit; and private capital inflow, on which the Government relies to balance its balance of payments books, is at a record $10.2 billion. We have increasing inflation over the past four years, no growth, exploding unemployment, record interest rates and an everdeteriorating balance of payments.

economy. Had this Government and the Attorney-General's Department, as requested by the Taxation Office, prosecuted as recommended by Brinsden J. back in 1975, these schemes would have been arrested. There would have been noneed to peddle sales tax increases as a means of indirectly combating tax avoidance. There would have been no need for the Government to renege on its promise to implement full tax indexation. There would have been no disproportionate rise in individual pay-as-you-earn tax receipts compared with non-pay-as-you-earn receipts and no decline in company tax as a percentage of total taxes. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) and the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) say that they are not responsible and the people of Australia are supposed to be satisfied with the prosecution of three minor officials in Perth. What about the Ministers who have let this enormous loss to the revenue take place, and what about the person who was the Crown Solicitor in those years and who is now head of the Attorney General's Department? What is the Government going to do about it? It will take no action at all against those Ministers and bureaucrats who are responsible.

That will not be before very long, I assure the House, because we are willing to fight an election, if the Government is game, at any time it likes. Our plans and policies include a return to centralised wage indexation, which will not only control unnecessary wage increases to very high income earners but also give justice to the one and a half million people who have not received any wage increase due to the Government's destruction of wage indexation early last year. We will go for real income tax reform-not fairy floss income tax reform, and a real crackdown on the tax rorts-and a fair system of financing State and local government through revenue sharing, through a return to the system of block and tied grants to finance essential social services such as health services, through a new consultative approach to Loan Council borrowing programs with the States and through the guarantee of total funds to the States. We will introduce a new health insurance scheme under which people and families will be asked to pay only 0.75 per cent of their taxable income instead of up to $16 a week which they pay now and which many families cannot afford under this Government. There will be new housing policies in contrast to the hopeless record of this Government in that area. I believe

Mr SPENDER (North Sydney) (8.45)-It is self-evident that Australia's long term prosperity as a trading nation depends greatly on the size and

profitability of our export industries. These industries can be damaged by factors beyond our control, factors such as the present drought and depressed international trading conditions. They can also be damaged by factors which are very much within our control. Of these, the two which are crucial to the prosperity and long term development of our export industries are the effect of industrial action on our capacity to deliver to importers on time and the cost competitiveness of those industries. It is beyond doubt that our world-wide reputation for industrial disruption, especially in the ports and in the major natural resources projects, and the industrial facts of life which have earned us this reputation, have greatly damaged our export industries and have caused our major trading partner, Japan, which accounts for around 27 per cent of our exports, to look elsewhere. This trend, if it continues will put our future prosperity at risk.
As a nation, we have a duty to the present and to the future to maximise our natural economic advantages. Those advantages are obvious: The wealth of our land, what lies in it, and what we can produce from it. The extent of our dependence on Australia's natural wealth is demonstrated by the fact that around 70 per cent of our export earnings come from agriculture and mining. It is notorious that over the last two decades the major source of growth of exports has been mining. Has that growth come too easily?
With the vast increase in mining developments over the last 20 years, the great interest that overseas investors have shown in those developments, and the high profits made by some in the good years, a kind of cargo-cult mentality has grown up in Australia. A good part of the community seems to hold the view that resource developments are a twentieth century money tree and that all one has to do to get money is shake the tree. Inherent in this mentality is the dangerously erroneous belief, to quote Mr Les Millen of the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd, that 'resource developments are so profitable that no demand, however extraordinary, need remain unsatisfied if only sufficient industrial muscle were applied'. It is almost an article of faith among many that Australia is so uniquely endowed with natural resources that foreigners, and especially the resource-poor Japanese, will continue to come to us no matter the cost, the inconvenience or the levels of industrial disruption they are forced to endure. Those who believe that resource developments are an endless source of money need look only at the recent catastrophic results of major Australia mining companies.

1206 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

Those who believe that we are so uniquely wealthy that foreigners will continue to beat a track to our sometimes closed door should look hard at a few facts of our recent industrial history. Firstly, figures produced by the Japanese finance ministry's customs section show that in the six months to the end of June 1982 Australia's share of Japan's coke and coal imports fell to 35.5 per cent compared to 47.7 per cent for the corresponding half year in 1981. In steaming coal, our share of Japan's imports fell from 52.5 per cent for the first half of the calendar year of 1981 to 43.4 per cent for the first half of 1982. In sum, in the first half of 1982 compared to the first half of 1981 we lost 12.2 per cent of Japan's total import market for coal and coke and 9.1 per cent of Japan's steaming coal market. Those losses were made good by our competitors, who will be determined to retain their increased share in this huge and lucrative market. In iron ore, as reported in the Australian Financial Review on 6 September, the story is the same. Our total share of the Japanese market has declined from a peak of 48.1 per cent in 1976 to 45.2 per cent last year, while our major competitor, Brazil, has increased its share of Japan's market to 23.8 per cent.
The last annual report of the ship owners Mutual Strike Insurance Association shows that insurance claims by ship owners for losses caused by industrial disputes in Australian waters exceeded the combined claims for the rest of the world. The figures for the 12 months to 31 January 1982 show that Australia accounted for an astonishing 51 per cent of all claims. Apart from Canada and the United Kingdom no other country accounted for more than 3 per cent. This was the fourth year in which claims for losses in Australian waters exceeded the combined claims for the rest of the world-testimony of some kind to the national urge to break records. If we keep breaking records in this area we may break the country as well. The direct costs to the Australian economy of this gold medal effort in industrial disruption were estimated by the Association to exceed $150m and the indirect costs could exceed $300m. The hidden costs are incalculable.
The gravity of the malaise affecting Australian ports was highlighted by the Minister for Transport and Construction (Mr Hunt) on 18 September 1981. In a statement made that day he pointed out that so far that year there had not been a single day in 1981 which had been completely free of strikes affecting at least some sector of shipping. but our international reputation for industrial instability is not based on port disputes

alone. In the period 1975 to 1979, of the 15 countries in the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development, Australia rated fourth in the incidences of stoppages in all industries and services. The implications of these figures are clear. It is much easier to acquire an international reputation for industrial instability and unreliability in meeting contracts than it is to rid oneself of such a reputation.
Contracts by Japanese companies to acquire what we mine involved the commitment of vast expenditures of money, often over long periods. Because Japan is virtually without any domestic reserves of raw material inputs which its industrial machine needs it is acutely conscious of its vulnerability to shipping delays caused by industrial unrest. There is no reason why the Japanese should have any special solicitude for our industria problems, nor why they should look at the delays that plague our export industries as being anything less than a mark of unreliability and irresponsibility. Given our recent track record it is only logical for Japan to move away from the very heavy reliance it has in the past placed on Australia as its primary source of raw materials for its industries.
Anyone who has visited Japan in the last 12 months and has talked to those involved in the import of those raw materials would have received, if he listened, a very clear message. With some incredulity that Australians could be so short sighted, that we could wantonly inflict such damage upon ourselves, Japanese industry has made a conscious decision to reduce its dependence on Australia for its raw materials. If this trend persists--and there is no reason why other importers of our raw materials should judge us differently-it must bring several consequences. In the short term, it will bring losses in export earnings. These losses are already reflected in our balance of trade figures which, excluding invisibles, have moved from a surplus which we enjoyed through the 1970s to a deficit of around $3.4 billion in the 1981-82 financial year-a deficit which in turn contributed to a current account deficit of just over $9 billion or around 6 per cent of gross domestic product for that financial year. Of course, in the medium and longer term we face cancelled and deferred developments, a reduced export trade and diminished national prosperity. A drastic reduction in the effect of industrial strife on our export industries is not merely desirable but essential to our survival as a prosperous nation. We are all involved-employers, employees, the Government, the unions and the nation as a whole. We

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1207

have a great deal to learn, a great distance to travel and little time.
The second of the factors that affect our export industry and one which we can control is cost competitiveness. It is directly and profoundly affected by the levels of wages and salaries. Wage restraint is central not only to our international competitiveness but also more generally to the prosperity and level of employment in our country now and in the future. By international standards and by any sensible reckoning we are living beyond our means. Recent wage settlements in the United States of America and West Germany demonstrate that employees in those countries are coming to realise the truth of the maxim: One man's wage rise is another man's job. In wage negotiations in West Germany this year unions agreed on pay increases which were less than the inflation rate. The same approach to wage increases has been shown by unions in the United States. The motor vehicle industry provides a striking example. Last April workers in the General Motors company voted to ratify a 2t year agreement under which the United Auto Workers Union agreed to forgo any pay increases during most of the life of the contract and to forgo progress towards a shorter working week. This followed a 'standstill' agreement with the Ford company and a 'give-back' agreement with the Chrysler company.
The need for restraint in wage demands and the acceptance in America of the necessity of increases less than the current rate of inflation were summed up by Mr Art Gundersheim, an official of the 400,000-strong American Amalgamated Clothing and Textile Workers Union, as reported in the Bulletin of 20 April this year. He said:
'The worker ultimately feels the availability of a job is more important than his income level. It's a reasonable, rational trade off. In periods of great economic difficulty there have always been concessions. In Chicago in the 60s I can remember workers at one company voting for a 10 per cent wage reduction '.
By contrast to the common sense and restraint summed up by Mr Gundersheim we evince a lemming-like, national determination to use today's pay rises to destroy tomorrow's jobs and opportunities.
The Australian Bureau of Statistics figures for the 1981-82 financial year show that average weekly earnings soared by 17.4 per cent-some 7 per cent above the rate of inflation and in real terms roughly 4 per cent above the increase in gross domestic product for that year. In the same period our rate of inflation was some 4 per cent above the average of our major OECD partners. This means that we are paying ourselves more

than we can afford. Given the harsh economic conditions in which we live it is easy to find examples of where this kind of conduct leads. I will give two examples. First, the latest national accounts show that gross operating surpluses of trading enterprises fell by 4 per cent in the December quarter and by 14 per cent in the March quarter. Secondly, a short time ago, and within the space of three days, some 850 jobs were lost in New South Wales following three separate management decisions. The most significant of those decisions was that of Amco Wrangler Ltd to take some of its operations off-shore. This decision is but one example of an ominous trend.
In present economic circumstances no country can afford wage and salary increases that constantly exceed the rate of inflation. Any trading nation that adopts such a course can be perfectly sure of the medium and long term consequences. For Australia those consequences will be: Decreasing international competitiveness which will affect not only our export industries but also our whole manufacturing base as we become more vulnerable to foreign penetration of home markets; the shedding of labour by employers at an increasingly rapid rate; companies and jobs going off-shore, which means a permanent loss of jobs; and insolvencies and company collapses. What can we do? In the Budget the Government offers Australians a clear opportunity and a clear choice-an opportunity for wage and salary restraint in the interests of all, and a choice between community acceptance by Australians of restraint, or a re-run of Whitlam economics, which is what the Australian Labor Party proposes.
The sincerity of the Government is evidenced by its profound commitment to meet the needs of the average and middle wage earner, the family man and those in need. This commitment is demonstrated in this year's Budget by such unarguable benefits which are to become effective on I November as: The increase in the tax free zone to $4,595; the reduction in the standard rate of income tax from 32c to 30c in the dollar; the lifting of the threshold from $17,884 to $~9,500 at which every extra taxable dollar attracts tax at the rate of 46c; the introduction of a 50 per cent increase in the family allowance for the first and second children; and the allowance of tax rebates at 30c in the dollar on interest payments over 10 per cent on housing loans up to $60,000. Other measures include: Assistance to pensioners and the handicapped; action already taken to counter tax avoidance and proposed action against tax evasion.
These are facts in place-commitments on the record. In return the Government asks, in the

1208 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

national interest, for wage and salary restraint and for no further reduction in hours of work. What is Labor's answer? Three days after the Budget was delivered the Leader of the Labor Party and the President of the Australian Council of Trade Unions issued a joint statement. Stripped of its rhetoric the statement has two key elements. The first key element is the adoption, in a centralised system of wage indexation, of full cost of living adjustments, including maintenance in real terms of existing levels of over-award payments, together with a variety of other benefits. Such an approach ignores world economic conditions, ignores Australia's economic condition, ignores the cost to the economy and to Australia's future prosperity and ignores the plight of the many workers who will be thrown out of employment as a direct result of the adoption of this policy. The second key element is the adoption of statutory controls on prices and profits, thinly disguised as a pricing authority with legislative criteria to assess price proposals of corporations within its jurisdiction. Those corporations-the large ones will be the pace setters-will be reassured to know that under Labor they will be allowed to earn reasonable profits-meaning, no doubt, what a Labor government thinks they should get. They would be unwise to hope for generosity, sympathy or even a basic understanding of the economic and financial constraints under which they operate.
Of Labor's proposals it can only be said either that the Labor Party operates in a cocoon of economic ignorance and believes that God made special rules for the Australian economy or that deliberately, and with full knowledge of the economic damage inherent in its proposals, it is willing to put Australians out of work, to destroy future employment prospects and to erode the progress of any future economic recovery-all this in pursuit of putting together a program that it hopes to be electorally palatable. The danger of the mixture of full indexation and other benefits and statutory controls on prices and profits was clearly expressed by Monsieur Jacques Larosier, the Managing Director of the International Monetary Fund, at a management symposium in Davos, Switzerland in March of this year. He said:
There is an urgent need for a more realistic alignment of wage demands with a prospective growth of real output and employment. The restoration of adequate profit margins-to provide the incentive and resources for growth-clearly requires that real wages be held below productivity gains for some time to come. A special problem arises in those countries where wage indexation schemes exist. Schemes of this sort are a many-headed evil.

It is precisely that evil that Labor proposes, and if Monsieur Larosier's criticisms had been specifically directed to Labor they could not have been more apt. The Executive Director of the International Monetary Fund realised the need for restraint. German and American unions accept that in present conditions-conditions not peculiar to Australia but prevalent throughout the worldrestraint and acceptance of less than what is needed to keep up with inflation is necessary. Mr Wran, the Premier of New South Wales, to his credit a few weeks ago publicly acknowledged the need for restraint, but not the Federal Labor Party. Labor's answer to our economic problems, and specifically to our need to regain cost competitiveness, amounts to higher wages, increased government expenditure, government job creation-a policy which entirely overlooks the fact that jobs have to be paid for out of taxes or out of profits and that in the long run permanent jobs can be created only out of profits-and control of prices. Controls on prices are the last things we need. They are an active incentive not to invest, not to create jobs and to go off-shore. They stifle initiative, enterprise and profits, and without a profitable employer one cannot have a profitable employee.
The ACTU and the major unions have an historic opportunity to work with the Government and to achieve restraint in regard to salaries, wages and other benefits in the interests of all Australians. To refuse to do so is to consign an increasingly large number of the Australian work force to the bitterness and misery of unemployment.
Mr COHEN (Robertson) (9.4)-This Budget was framed with only one objective-to help the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) win an election this year. It is the most cynical Budget. Mr COHEN-Anybody who takes the time and trouble to examine these statistics will see a clear picture of the Government's failure. The inflation rate in 1975-76 was 13 per cent-these are the Parliamentary Library figures-and it is now around II per cent. There has been a slight reduction in inflation. The level of unemployment in 1975-76 was 275,900, and the latest figures show that it is over 470,000 and rising. The growth in the gross non-farm product area in the first three years of the Labor Government was 5.2 per cent, 4.2 per cent, one per cent and 2 per cent. Last year the figure was 2.3 per cent. We are told that there will be no growth at all this year. That is the result of the seven years of restraint and selfdenial that has been asked of the people of Australia.
We are facing an economic disaster. In this Budget the Prime Minister is saying this: 'We, the Liberal Party and the National Country Party, have failed miserably to fulfil any of our promises. Although we failed, it is not our fault; the fault lies with the world recession'. State governments, trade unions, and even the weather are blamed for Prime Minister Fraser's mistakes. The one thing our beloved Prime Minister is totally consistent

about is never accepting responsibility for anything bad that happens in this country. He is responsible only when something good happens. This Budget is an admission of total failure. Having admitted that the Government has failed and that he does not know what to do about the state of the economy, the Prime Minister, in a most incredibly cynical manner, offers a range of goodies to the voters to tempt them to trust him once again. Let me assure the Prime Minister that the old saying-I hate using cliches but one has to be used here-'You can fool some of the people some of the time but you cannot fool all of the people all of the time' was never more appropriate than at this moment.
The Australian voter has a very high threshold of pain when it comes to Australian politicians, particularly conservative politicians, but that threshold was reached just a few months ago. At the last election enough Australian voters still believed that Malcolm Fraser had something up his sleeve besides his arm. They believed him when he said the resources boom which he, the Treasurer (Mr Howard), and the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) trumpeted from one end of the country to another was to eventuate. Enough people were still mesmerised by the pots of gold-the Australian EI Dorado; the Liberal Utopia-to give him and his colleagues just one more chance. The Liberals well know that the result of the last election, their 23 seat majority, was very much closer if they look at it in terms of votes and not seats. Nearly all donkey votes in some extremely close results in a number of very marginal seats favoured the Government, but the Government knows as well as we do that a further uniform swing of a little over one per cent would have seen it in opposition. Since then the number of our seats has increased by one and the Government's majority has slipped even further.
The simple fact-I think more and more Government members are reaching this conclusion-is that time has run out. There is a point in the life of every government when the voter no longer believes in anything that it says. No government in Australian history has been given a fairer go than the Fraser Government. Three times the people of Australia have given it a mandate to deliver the promises that the Prime Minister made in 1975. For five years it has had massive majorities in both Houses. For the past two years it has had a comfortable majority in the House of Representatives, whilst in the Senate-I think I am right-only two Bills have been twice rejected. No one in his wildest flights of fantasy could claim that the rejection of either of these Bills has seriously affected the Government's

economic policies. Therefore the Government cannot use the excuse that it has not had all the opportunities. It has had solid control of both Houses.

Whenever the election is held, be it November this year or Mayor December next year, the people will deliver their verdict clearly and unequivocally. The only question now is the size of the Australian Labor Party's majority. Let us all as Australians determine to restore prosperity, defeat inflation and provide jobs for all. The Liberal and National Country Parties have prepared a far-reaching programme to restore prosperity and to give effect to our philosophy of freedom, opportunity and concern for the individual.

Mr DRUMMOND (Forrest) (9.25)~ This is a Budget that the electors of Australia have been waiting for. It is a Budget for families. It leaves more money in the pockets of the taxpayers. For the first time it gives average Australians the incentive to invest in their own and their country's future. I am sure that a lot of people are saying 'It's about time too' on learning of the cuts in income tax. Tax cuts are what the Liberal Party stands for~letting people decide how their earnings should be spent rather than governments grabbing the money and doing it for them. Even critics of the Government have to admit that these cuts are substantial~an extra $7.65 a week for the pay-as-you-earn taxpayer on average earnings of $344 and the raising of the standard rate to $19,500 so that 75 per cent of taxpayers will pay no more than 30c in the dollar.
It is equally commendable that a special emphasis has been given to assisting those people who have come to feel that they are being left behind~the single income family with a mortgage. The higher spouse rebate, the increased family allowance payments and the extension of interest rate relief to all home buyers will together ease the burden of costs on these families. This does not mean that some of us on the Government back bench will not continue to advocate the splitting of sole breadwinners' incomes for tax assessment as the best long term means of securing tax justice for the single income family, but this Budget is a big step forward.
In the medium term, lower taxation and an increased family allowance give a real incentive for the wage earner and the trade unionist to reject higher wage demands. I appreciate very well that a family struggling with a mortgage and a high personal tax bite will not appreciate the finer points of the need for wage restraint. Now, however, the pressure should be reduced with a tax cut having the same effect as a wage rise. More and more electors are now realising that wages

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1213

cannot keep rising so far in advance of productivity -least of all when growth is sluggish. High wage demands not only make our industry uncompetitive but also simply price labour out of the market. People become too expensive to employ. Despite the reception given by the Australian Council of Trade Union leadership, which seems more worried about the seizure of the initiative by the Liberal Government than concerned for the long term good of its members, I am hopeful that the rank and file unionists, the very blokes whom this Budget sets out to help, will take a different view. If with their co-operation the so-called trade-off works it will be a very strong blow against inflation.
There are, of course, more long term considerations that are equally important. Within the constraints of economic responsibility every Liberal National Country Party Government has a duty to work towards the lowering of direct personal tax. We stand committed to maximising incentive and freedom of choice. Shifting some of the tax load to indirect taxation, as this Budget does, gives the taxpayer that extra discretion as to how his dollar will be spent. I have always been wary of sudden lurches into massive indirect taxation of the kind that could disadvantage the low income earner and hamper the small businessman with the weight of administrative bureaucracy. Hence, last year many electors of Forrest were concerned that the projected rise in sales tax was not matched by a reduction in direct taxation. This time, however, there is no denying that the modest rise in sales tax will be more than covered by the cut in income tax and the increases in social security payments.
It does not hurt to point out that Australia in the last available survey of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development countries ranked low in the scale of the proportion of gross domestic product consumed by indirect taxes-fifteenth on a list of 22,8 per cent of GOP against a 10 per cent average. Direct taxation, however, accounted for 12l per cent of GOP. There is in my opinion some room for a gradual shift from direct to indirect taxation provided always that it will not increase the overall tax burden. At this juncture it is appropriate to stress an important advantage of indirect taxation-that it is harder to evade. So often we overlook the fact that the greatest revenue loss comes not through the notorious tax avoidance schemes; it comes through straight concealment of income in small amounts but on a massive scale-the underground economy. On one estimate, this was as much as 10 per cent of gross domestic product. If

on both sides of the political divide we are genuinely concerned about tax evasion we cannot afford to condemn the idea of a gradual shift to more indirect taxation. It makes sense to me to curtail the underground economy in this way rather than to pursue thousands of tradesmen and contractors over concealed cash income.
There is a third main source of tax revenue, that is, tax on capital. This Budget is free of that blight, and the people I represent will say 'Amen' to that. As a Liberal I am totally opposed to any seizure by the state of the assets of a citizen and the crippling of his economic liberty that can only
erode his freedom. It is one of the great differences that divide liberalism and socialism. I remind the House that it was this Government which in the last Parliament removed the cruellest of all taxes on capital-probate. The inspiration might have come from Queensland, but it was the Fraser Government that abolished it at the national level and thus thwarted the attempt of the Cain Government in Victoria to impose it on the people of Victoria. I will always be proud to have supported a government that has stood up for genuine principles on this matter. It will stand as a blot on the records of previous coalition governments that they tolerated the evil of death duties. People remember well the effect of probate on the farm and the small businessman, the intrusion of the tax gatherer at a time of private grief.
We should remember that when this House removed probate in 1978 the Australian Labor Party was not prepared to vote against such a popular move, yet Opposition speakers made it clear that they held strong commitment to both this and other forms of capital taxation. We heard then and we hear now complaints that Australia is one of the few Western nations without a significant tax on capital. Frankly, this is a fact that
makes so many of us proud to be Australians. New Zealand is one of the few countries which are free of poisonous snakes. By the same logic, I
suppose, there are people there bemoaning that fact and wanting to import a few.
I wish to make special mention of an important innovation contained in this Budget, that is, the rebate on the first $1,000 of dividend income, which is a saving to the investor of $300 in taxation. The anomaly of taxing company profits twice, first through company tax and then as income in the hands of the shareholder, has been highlighted in the report of the Campbell Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System and in earlier inquiries. In terms of equity, such double taxation cannot be removed entirely as the practical effect of doing that would leave some fairly affluent people paying no tax at all.

1214 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

The Government, to its great credit, has recognised that the small investor is getting no encouragement to put his savings into Australian enterprises as opposed to real estate. It has for a long time been of concern to me that the average wage earner has thought of the stock market only in terms of speculative capital gain and not as a means of steady income support. There is, of course, a place for some speculation in helping to finance the work of oil and mineral exploration, but for the health of our economy it is essential that more small investors should have a stake in major national enterprises and prosper with them.
Some would say that tax exemption for the first $1,000 of dividend income is not much, but this, nevertheless, ranks as a vital first step. For the first time the small investor has his foot in the
door because a Liberal government is acting according to essential Liberal principles. Many of those who will benefit from the rebate are age pensioners. As a society, we must encourage people to provide for their own retirement if we want to avoid a massive burden on taxpayers in
years to come. This reform is a part of such a process whereby many people with quite modest incomes might now begin to build up investment portfolios which, by the time of retirement, could return a worthwhile supplement to normal superannuation, thus reducing their need for direct income support through the age pension.
This leads me to the well publicised aspect of the Budget, that is, the assistance it gives to the elderly. Those of us who try to listen carefully to our electors have realised for some time that the concern of many retired people is not so much the level of the pension, now that the Fraser Government's policy of indexation has been operating for
some time, but rather the amount of other income they could receive before losing fringe benefits or paying tax. The rise in the other income limit, the raising of the tax threshold, and the pensioner's tax rebate will all help to lessen these anxieties. Of particular concern have been the modest, fixed
superannuation payments that could actually disadvantage some age pensioners. The effect of these reforms will be to allow pensioners a total income of some $7,000 before tax is payable, to which we can add a further $1,000 if such income is obtained from dividends. This will enable a pensioner couple to receive $90 per week in other income before losing fringe benefits. In brief, we can applaud the incentive given to elderly people to retain private income.
Of the many changes in the social security policy and payments I would like to single out the new proposal for a family income supplement for the low income family which does not receive

social security assistance. I am convinced that such families very often live in rural electorates and that they have found themselves in increasing difficulty in recent years. When this scheme becomes operative next year that extra $10 per dependent child will ~ertainly help these family budgets. Taking a longer view, I consider that we have to get to grips with a fairer division of welfare expenditure. Whilst I am glad that the major spending increases in the social security area are geared towards the family, I cannot rest entirely easily with the fact that the effective increase in total spending for the Department of Social Security has risen some 15.4 per cent to 28.2 per cent of total Budget outlays. On both sides of the House we need to look at ways whereby some proportion of family allowances is directed away from the more affluent to those in need. This is a lot harder than it sounds. For a major saving we would need to place the income test at average weekly earnings which would hardly be a popular measure. Nevertheless, it would seem that some kind of carefully graduated income test must one day be used if we want to assist the low income family without adding to the tax burden and wrecking the economy.
I stand firmly behind the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) in his contention that any attack on poverty must not be made in the universal manner of increasing benefits right across the board, which is the essence of slothful, bureaucratic socialism. In calling for selective welfare to tackle what is being perceived as a growing problem, I do not see why the advocates of such a solution need be confined to the non-Labor side of politics. The honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) just instanced that type of approach in his speech. Selective welfare should appeal to anyone looking for a fairer means to relieve poverty. In the last few years, reality has dawned on the Liberal Party at the grass roots level that universal welfare can result only in national bankruptcy. Such reality may not, as yet, have caught up with the ALP. Certainly the Family Income Supplement Scheme, whatever its teething problems may be, has the virtue of trying to reach a special area of need on a selective basis.
As the representative of a rural electorate I appreciate the feeling that there is no special good news for the farm sector in this Budget other than the wiping off of the beef producers' debt for the brucellosis eradication campaign. Nonetheless, farmers are also taxpayers, family people and investors and their position can hardly be viewed in isolation. Total Budget outlays in the agricultural sector of $31 Om show an increase of some 40 per

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1215

cent on the last financial year. I am also very confident that should the drought worsen on a national basis, as it is in the process of doing, unfortunately, the Federal Government has demonstrated that it stands ready to respond. The response it has given is very humane and justified. Furthermore, the farm sector remains hopeful that the Government will resist added protection demands which weaken our competitive position on overseas markets. I have some reservations as to the operation of the Diesel Fuel Rebate Scheme on the grounds that it may involve cumbersome paper work without any great saving to the taxpayer. I also note comments by the fishing industry that such large users of diesel fuel would be carrying a major additional cost while awaiting the rebate. If indeed the new scheme proves unsatisfactory I would hope that speedy alterations might be made.
The increase in the retention allowance for private companies from 70 per cent to 80 per cent is welcome news for the small business sector and continues the process begun by this Government three years ago when the allowance was raised from 50 per cent. I am hopeful that small business will benefit from increased consumer spending that will more than offset the rise in sales tax and excise. One group of small businessmen in my electorate of Forrest who perhaps have cause to be satisfied with this Budget are the wine growers who have avoided the introduction of a wine excise. I am delighted that the Fraser Government has again shown the foresight to avoid such an imposition on an industry that is largely still establishing itself-that is, within the electorate of Forrest. It has been demonstrated in the south west of Western Australia that we can grow wines equal to those produced in any part of Australia, indeed the world. But it is still a fledgling industry in my electorate.
Finally, both as the member for an electorate where tourism is a major industry and as Chairman of the Government Members' Committee on Tourism I welcome the continuing support for the tourist industry by this Government. Not only has a record $9.7m been allocated for overseas promotion by the Australian Tourist Commission but the industry incentive package of last month extended a depreciation allowance of 2t per cent to all buildings in the tourist industry. This continues the initial advance made three years ago when the allowance was granted for accommodation buildings. In the same package the Export Market Development Grants Scheme was amended to raise the maximum grant to tourist operators to $200,000, to waive the minimum spending requirements of $5,000 and to extend eligibility.

The fact that tourism is no longer the poor relation of more favoured industries is a sign that in this field, as in many others, the Fraser Government is keeping well up front with reality.
The Government has set the scene. The Government continues to give this country sound, honest management. Basically the future is in our own hands. It it up to us, every member of society, as to how much he or she is prepared to contribute as against how much he or she wishes to take out. I believe it would require very little effort on everyone's behalf to turn our economy into a vibrant, healthy, fully employed economy with a secure future for all of us.
Mr MOUNTFORD (Banks) (9.43)-1 am pleased to join the debate on Appropriation Bill (No. I) which forms the major thrust of the Government's Budget presented to the Parliament three weeks ago. The 1982 Budget is a reversal of recent previous Budgets in that it proposes to loosen the tight monetary rein for politically expedient reasons in an attempt to give the Government the advantage in the forthcoming election. But the Australian people have been down this road before. They have experienced similar deceptions in the 1977 Budget which was brought down only months before an election was called. The Government offered tax cuts, it maintained tax indexation and offered a range of goodies. These supposedly responsible economic managers estimated that the cost of all those Budget measures would lead to a Budget deficit of only $1.4 billion. Three months later the Government went to the polls and won. Almost immediately after it became apparent that the Budget had blown out. The deficit was heading towards $2.5 billion. The Government had given the electorate the goods before the election but had wrongly calculated the costs.
In the 1978 Budget the Government took back its tax concessions, it backed down on its promised full tax indexation and it introduced the oil levy so that in one year the Government's revenue from oil and liquefied petroleum gas jumped from $800m to $1.3 billion. Medibank was dismantled and health insurance became more expensive, excise taxes were increased and there were further increases in sales tax and other indirect taxes. The Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) estimated that these measures put an extra 2 per cent on our inflation figure each year. People were told to tighten their belts, that life was not going to be easy. They experienced further deception in 1980 when the Government fabricated the resources boom. Suddenly they were told that Australia was once again the lucky country and that all Australians could expect to

1216 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

share the benefits of exceptional growth and national development. But the resources boom collapsed soon after the 1980 elections. Many big projects were either shelved or cancelled, for example, the Rundle shale oil project, the Alcan, Lochinvar and Portland aluminium smelters and the now ironic proposal to expand the Newcastle steel works.
The Australian people are awake to the Fraser Government's not having a sound economic framework as the basis for its policy decisions. They are awake to the reality of the Government's lurching from one election to the next, trying to bring down the policies which may get it re-elected but which will not get Australia out of its chronic economic malaise. I think the Australian people are awake to the fact that the Budget now before Parliament is another cynical exercise in political expediency.
The scenario for this situation was devised some months ago. First, we saw the Government introduce some incentives for the business community by way of increased depreciation allowances and plant investment allowances, et cetera, prior to the Budget so that maximum publicity could be obtained for those measures. I shall deal with these measures a little later. Secondly, we have the present Budget which provides, on the face of it, a basketful of goodies for the middle income earner, the swinging voter and some relief for our pensioner population. It is a Budget designed for an early election because of the handouts it proposes and because it lacks any provision for a reversal of the present poor economic condition of our country. It is a Budget which will do nothing to get the economy moving again. By the admission of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) we will have virtually no real growth and increased unemployment. It is a hotch-potch recipe for economic
disaster for Australia in the long term.
As the Leader of the Opposition has stated, in this Budget the Government has again used rubbery and downright deceptive figures. By understating expenditure and overstating income the Government is attempting to show that the deficit will be only $1,674m. This is an act of downright deception which will be seen by the community in its true light at the end of this financial year. That is another reason why the Government is so eager to have an election. It could then do what it did in 1978 after the election in 1977-take back the tax cuts six months after they were given. Even the so-called basket of goodies is deceptive. Three major problems facing my constituents in the electorate of Banks-and I am sure facing most Australians-have, according to the Treasurer, been addressed in this Budget. They are taxation,

housing and unemployment. A closer look at the Government's Budget measures shows that the basket of goodies has a false bottom or does not address the real problem.
First, I will deal with the tax measures. What are these tax measures? The Government has said that from November 1982 it will increase the tax threshold from $4,195 to $4,595 and the 46c tax threshold from $17,894 to $19,500. As well, the 32c in the dollar tax scale will be reduced to 30c. Accompanying these changes indirect taxes will be increased. On the face of it the measures, excluding the indirect tax increases, seem favourable. For a long time the Australian Labor Party has been urging the Government to grant some compensation. In context, however, the Government offers very little to most Australians. I have two major areas of complaint. First, the measures offer only little more than what full indexation would have given them. Secondly, it offers only a small reversal of the seven years of a regressive tax system.
On the first point, the tax concessions announced with such grandiosity in reality amount to only a little over what the taxpayer would have been entitled to under promised automatic tax indexation. The cost of the concessions in 1982-83 is estimated to be $1.6 billion, whereas the cost of full indexation is $1.5 billion. The Liberal Party praised the merits of tax indexation in 1975 when it promised to reform the tax system. Tax indexation it said would make government more honest with taxpayers' money because government would no longer be able to rely on the secret tax increase of inflation. These words were spoken by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in 1975. Yet over the proceeding years that commitment was rarely met. For the six financial years from 1976-77 to 1981-82 inclusive full tax indexation was applied only twice. In three of the remaining years half tax indexation was applied and in 1979-80 there was no tax indexation at all. Seen in this context, this Budget's tax concessions do not give much to the taxpayer; they simply stop the practice of automatically taking the hidden tax increases that inflation permits. I seek leave to have a table showing benefits of the Budget measures incorporated in Hansard.

Mr MOUNTFORD-I thank the House. Over the past seven years the lower the income of a taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two children, as shown in the table, the greater proportion of his income he has had to pay in tax. For example, a person who now earns $12,062 pays 6.4 per cent of his income in tax. In 1975-76 he paid 1.6 per cent. The proportion has increased in this period by 300 per cent. I compare this with a person earning $36,187. He now pays 30 per cent of his income in tax. In 1975-76 he paid 29.3 per cent.
The increase is only 2.3 per cent. It is only when we get into even higher income groups that that percentage has decreased. We now see that when
the Prime Minister said in the 1975 election campaign that his Government would reduce the tax burden he must have meant that the burden would be reduced for only a very select group of taxpayers, that is, the richer ones.
So much for the Budget's tax cuts. What about the housing measures? The Government has said that this Budget addresses the housing problem. I dispute this claim for several reasons. First, what is the housing problem? There are three main groups in our community who are experiencing a housing problem. Firstly, there are those people who are in the private rental market and are unlikely ever to be in a position of purchasing their own homes. This is the long term housing problem which faces the lower income groups in society. Secondly, there are those people who are in the private rental market and who are likely to be able to afford their own homes. These are the people currently faced with the deposit gap. Thirdly, there are those people who have already committed themselves to the purchase of houses but who are currently experiencing cash flow problems as a result of rising interest rates. This group, comprising primarily middle income earners, is in need of short term assistance.
What has the Government done for the first group? All the Budget does for this group, which makes up 25 per cent of the population, is to allocate $IOm in rent relief assistance. This compares with the $562m package to home purchasers who represent approximately 25 per cent of the Australian population. The Government has also increased supplemenatary assistance to pensioners in rented accommodation but by a mere $2 a week. Rentals are rising at an annual rate of more than $10 a week. If the Government were serious in alleviating the housing problem for this group it would extend considerably the access to public rental housing. The Government-I give it credit for this-has increased the welfare housing share in this Budget but over the past eight years in real terms the money it has spent on welfare

housing has declined, in 1982-83 prices, from $975m in 1974-75 to $290m in 1982-83. The cumulative real reduction in public housing funding over that period is a staggering $3,740m.
The second group comprises those people who are unable to bridge the deposit gap, that is, the people who are eligible for the maximum home loan which is far exceeded by the cost of a home. Only two courses of action are open to a person in this position. Either he rents accommodation until he can save the deposit or he obtains a second mortgage. For the person who takes the first option this Budget offers little to no help. He will still be paying $90 to $100 a week in rent and if he has enough money to save he will pay tax on the interest his savings earn. The person who takes the second option will receive some assistance through the interest rebate, which I will deal with later. But his more basic problem, that of having to obtain a second mortgage, is not recognised at all. By tightening the money supply the Government is, in fact, making it harder for him to get finance.
The third group comprises the people who purchased houses recently and are now finding it difficult to cope with interest rate rises. Many people within my electorate are experiencing such a problem. In recent years several new subdivisions have been opened up to home buyers in the Milperra, Padstow Heights and Lugarno areas. Those who thought they could afford to buy homes in early 1980 have had to evaluate their positions. Since then savings banks' interest rates have risen from 9.5 per cent to 13.5 per cent. This 4 per cent increase has meant that repayments on a $30,000 loan have risen from $262 a month to $350 a month-an annual increase of $1,056. Many home owners are struggling to maintain their mortgages. Statistics on mortgage defaults from the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation show
that between 1970-71 and 1979-80 mortgage defaults have increased from 4 per cent of total premiums to 35 per cent. The people in this group get perhaps the most benefit from this Budget-a rebate of 30c in the dollar on interest payments above a rate of IO per cent on loans up to $60,000 no matter what their incomes or the sources of their loans. But whilst the measure is welcome, it goes only a fraction of the way to reducing the $1,056 in extra repayments that a person who purchased a home in 1980 with a loan of $30,000 is enduring. Under the March scheme the maximum rebate he could anticipate was $300 this year. Under the Budget rebate scheme this amount will be $377.50. The measure also assists home buyers who may not be in need of government assistance at all. Over half of the estimated

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1982-83

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1219

cost of the interest rebate will go to home buyers with incomes in excess of 150 per cent of average weekly earnings.
In the short time. I have left in this debate I want to say something about what this Budget offers for the unemployed. There has been a very real deterioration of the employment market in this country. In July this year there was no employment growth. The unemployment rate was

6.6 per cent. It is expected to top 10 per cent in February 1983. In my electorate of Banks many firms are literally going to the wall. I have prepared a table showing some of these companies which I seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard.

Mr MOUNTFORD-I thank the House. As this table shows, retrenchments have grown dramatically this year in my electorate, hitting not just young apprentices but also middle aged bread winners who sometimes have been with a company for many years. The list I have given is by no means absolute. It records only significant retrenchments by major employers. The number of small scale retrenchments is not known, but, according to the manager of the local Commonwealth Employment Service at Revesby, it is very considerable. Jobs are being eradicated from industry, not temporarily, but on a permanent basis. We will not get them back.
What is the Fraser Government doing about it? Firstly, it announced with much bravado an investment package for industry. The package, according to the Treasurer, will assist industry with investment and will thereby retain jobs and increase employment. I contend that it will do the opposite. The package, in fact, will encourage industry to invest in capital intensive, not labour intensive, measures. Clearly, the Government has not even read its own commissioned report on technological change. This report clearly indicates the transformation of industry from labour intensive investment to capital intensive investment. The Government's package simply speeds up this process. Let me add that I concede that the

new technologies may be necessary if Australian industry is to remain competitive. In fact I believe the Government is not doing enough to encourage research and development of our own technology or to back our local inventions with venture capital.

Mr MOUNTFORD (Banks) (10.0)- Secondly, the Government delivers a Budget which has a no-growth strategy as its basis. By budgeting for a $240m domestic surplus the Government is taking money out of the pockets of wage and salary earners and out of the profits of firms facing severe liquidity and investment problems. The foreign deficit does nothing to generate

1220 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Adjournment

jobs in Australia. By restricting the growth in the money supply to 9 per cent to II per cent, just enough to accommodate the expected inflation rate of 10.75 per cent, it provides no capacity for growth. To hold unemployment at its present level we must achieve an annual growth of at least 3 per cent. This means aiming at a domestic deficit and an increase in the money supply-the reverse of the Fraser Government's policies.
Thirdly, the Government says that it is increasing its manpower training programs by 2I per cent for 1982-83. On the face of it, it is a welcome increase but when seen in the context of the overalI Budget, which forecasts no employment growth in 1982-83, it is ludicrous. The Government can talk all it likes about grandiose schemes to retrain the unemployed, but the reality is that the jobs are not there even when they gain the skilIs. Fourthly, and it is hardly worth mentioning, the Government has increased the unemployment benefits in the Budget. The increase of $4 a week in benefits for the under-18-year-olds represents an II per cent increase in seven years. For those unemployed who are over 18 years their benefit of $64.40 is stilI $26.70 below the poverty line. These measures are a total insult to those unemployed because of the Government's own economic policies.
In conclusion, let me say that I am disappointed with the Government's strategies in managing the Australian economy. Where we should have seen increasing productivity and employment, we have instead seen low growth and increasing unemployment. The Government has blindly folIowed the United States of America and the United Kingdom down the Friedman economic path of recession and depresson. The world is now on the brink of economic depression because the major countries of the West have pursued wrong policies-policies that were based on political expediency. In Australia we need a government of vision, a government that has faith in the future, a government that is willing to forgo short term, stop-go policies of expediency in favour of long term economic stability which wilI improve our people's standard of living. We need a leader who wilI unite our people in a true nationalistic spirit.
