Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (10.4)-1 draw to the attention of the House a Press statement issued by the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) on 27 August 1982 in which he indicated the Government's support for the proposition of greater uniformity in liability in repatriation legislation. He indicated that it was the Government's intention that repatriation legislation be amended to provide greater uniformity in Commonwealth liability to provide benefits to veterans and their dependants. He specificalIy indicated that the proposed amendment was designed to remove existing anomalies within the legislation which deprived veterans, particularly those who had taken part in the Vietnam war, of entitlements equivalent to those for those veterans who had fought in earlier conflicts on behalf of this nation. Of course, that was basicalIy an endorsement of the policies and of the views which had been argued for and on behalf of the Opposition in this House in April of this year. Not being one who would ever want to look a gift horse in the mouth and not wanting to be churlish about this matter, 1 would have been perfectly happy to compliment the Minister and the Government on this much needed amendment had it not been for what seemed to me to be an exercise in politicking by the Minister at the end of his Press statement. 1 rather suspect that he said what he did at the end of the statement out of a feeling of guilt. He said:
The Government has needed no prodding on this issue and it is significant that the legislation changes it proposes are broader than those proposed by other parties.
To say that the Government needed no prodding on this issue is, I suggest, an indication of the guilty mind of the Minister. In March of this year there was before this House a Repatriation Amendment BilI. An amendment was moved on that occasion by my colIeague the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) which was seconded by me. That amendment contemplated the very amendment which is now contemplated by the Minister. On that occasion the Minister handling the BilI on behalf of the Government in this House indicated that the Government could not accept that amendment. A division was calIed

Adjournment

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1221

and every honourable member on the Government side of the House followed the view expressed by the Government and cast his or her vote against any changes in the legislation. I indicated that I thought this was the kind of amendment which should have attracted support from both sides of the House and I urged the Government to examine what seemed to me to be the justice of that claim while the legislation passed between this place and the Senate.
The Bill was debated in the Senate in April 1982 and the very same amendment was moved in the Senate. On that occasion Government supporters again used their numbers to reject any such proposal. Some four months later the Government has suddenly changed its mind on the very issue which its members were not prepared to consider appropriate or just in March or April of this year. Yet the Minister says: 'We did not need any prodding'. What caused this change was the decision of the Vietnam Veterans Association to campaign in marginal seats for what was plain justice. I commend it for its actions and decision. I salute the result it has already achieved for its members from its action. I can only hope that the Minister, having conceded the justice of this occasion, will now consider the claim for further justice and grant these people the judicial inquiry they have sought for so long.
Mr JULL (Bowman) (1O.8)-On 26 March 1964 a British court in Aylesbury, Buckinghamshire, convicted 10 men for their involvement in the £2.5m 1963 British mail train robbery. They were sentenced by Mr Justice Edmund Davies on 16 April of that year to prison terms ranging up to 30 years. Explaining the severity of the sentences Davies said to those he was sentencing:
Potential criminals . . . dazzled by the size . . . of the prize must be taught that punishment will be proportionately greater; let us clear any romantic notions . . . it is . . . a sordid crime of violence inspired by vast greed . . . it would be an affront if you were at liberty in the near future to enjoy these ill-gotten gains.
Those convicted included Ronald Arthur Biggs, then 34 years, a carpenter. Biggs, a member of the gang that executed that robbery, escaped from Wandsworth prison in London on 8 July 1966 with three other prisoners. Of course, we know the record of his activities in Australia where he lived for some time under an alias. We know that the police suspect Biggs of having been involved in robberies in Australia. We know that he fled Australia leaving his wife and family to fend for themselves. We know of his history in the West Indies and in South America. We know that he fathered a child to a Brazilian woman so that he

could remain in Brazil and we know that the many attempts that were made by the British authorities to have him extradited to Great Britain have failed.
In recent days we have seen in newspapers in Australia the same Ronald Arthur Biggs advertising a particular brand of coffee. The Federation of Australian Commercial Television Stations refused to accept the commercials that featured Ronald Biggs selling that coffee, but unfortunately full page advertisements have been run in some newspapers suggesting that people buy the brand of coffee Cafe de Rio. I just hope that when people take notice of that advertisement and think that it is very clever-obviously it was a good idea on the part of the advertising agencythey will remember that the guard on that train lost his life to make Biggs notorious to an extent that he could be hired by an Australian advertising agency, no doubt at very great cost, to make this commercial.
We know that Ronald Biggs has featured in Press advertisements and television advertisements which were never shown. The latest estimate that I can gain on what he may have been paid to undertake this promotion was in excess of $A35,000. I believe that the advertising agency should be condemned and that the manufacturers of that brand of coffee should also be condemned for even considering having this man involved in the promotion of their product. I make a plea to the people of Australia: The next time they are in a supermarket and see the coffee brand Cafe de Rio, avoid it like the plague.

Mr ADERMANN (Fisher) (10.17)-1 desire to raise a matter which has caused me quite some

concern and on which 1have had a number of representations. The alert and the observant will have already noted that 1 have asked a question
on this matter. Student benefit guidelines propose that no unemployment benefit payments will be made to tertiary students during university and college recess. Those guidelines contemplate a recess of two or at the most three months. 1 agree with that general principle, although I recognise that there can be difficulties.
I wish to raise particularly the case of Griffith University. We have a situation there that 1 believe is most unfortunate. The Griffith University is very close to the Commonwealth Games Com-
plex. The campus and the buildings are being appropriated for the purposes of the Commonwealth Games. For that reason the educational year started early at Griffith and it will end this month, September. 1 believe that there will be no resumption at that university until the end of
February; so the recess will last for about five months. This poses a very great difficulty for those students who are not able to get the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowance. Vacation jobs are very difficult to obtain. The observation of the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs and Minister Assisting the Minister for Social Security (Mr Wilson) this morning that the longer recess will provide an opportunity for students to obtain vacation jobs to supplement their incomes and to gain experience is fine but it is not realistic because jobs are just not easy to obtain, particularly in the electorate which 1 represent. If they were easy to obtain, 1 would have no complaint and I would not be raising this matter in the House tonight. In the cases that have come to my notice, the parents' resources are severely strained. Indeed, the students who have spoken to me feel very poorly about putting so much of a strain on their parents for so long a time. Students continuing their studies are in a position that I believe was not comprehended when the guidelines were set. Such a long period of recess was not envisaged. This is not the fault of the students. The action was taken to hold the Commonwealth Games and the Commonwealth Government has supported requests for assistance from the State Government and the Brisbane City Council. We have given that help. But continuing students, through no fault of their own, are having their semesters altered and their recess elongated. They face a serious problem. They are like the ham in the sandwich. I appeal to the Minister to look very closely at the problem which has arisen to see whether there is any element of injustice and whether there are grounds for considering a variation of the guidelines for the continuing students

Adjournment

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1223

at Griffith University. This year they have a long recess and will be in a once only position of being unable to get vacation jobs. The difficulties are serious.
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (1O.20)-Five weeks ago my wife and I spent a few days in Kakadu National Park.
Mr Les Johnson-How did it go?
Dr THEOPHANOUS-It was very interesting, as the honourable member will hear in a moment. We were interested to look at several of the issues involved there: Firstly, the situation of the Aboriginal people; secondly, the effect of uranium mining on the area; and thirdly, what was actually happening to the park. There is a conflict of interests in that area. From my observations and from discussions I had with, for example, the rangers in the park, Aboriginal people, and the people involved in uranium mining I believe that this conflict will become more accentuated in the future. There seem to be so many different concepts of the future of that area and what will happen to it. I wish to deal firstly with the proposed uranium mines at Jabiluka and Koongarra, which are massive projects. One of those projects has been approved by the Northern Territory Government. That approval was obtained after supposed extensive discussions with the Aboriginal people in the area. In my discussions with them. I found that tactics of intimidation and attempted bribery had been used over those people to attempt to force them into a position where they finally gave way to the demands from the companies involved in uranium mining at Jabiluka.
It is interesting to contrast that situation with the situation in an area further south but still in the park-the Koongarra mine. The Aboriginal people there, organised by the Gagudju Association, have rejected the proposals for uranium mining in the area and in rejecting those proposals they have created a crisis for the Northern Territory Government. To this stage the Northern Territory Government has been able to use one technique or another to force the Aboriginal people to accept these kinds of mining leases. Some of the reasons why the Aboriginal people rejected the Koongarra mine apply to all of the mines in that area. In fact, some of those reasons apply to the problems faced in the whole park area. The first and major reason is that the Koongarra mine is close to a major sacred site, the home of the lightning god of the Aboriginal people in the area. Given that situation, they are understandably very reluctant to allow mining in that place. If one visits that area one finds that is an absolutely

beautiful part of Australia that would be totally ruined by a uranium mine, especially when one takes into account the weather conditions that exist and the roads and other works that would have to be built.
Already in the middle of the Kakadu Park is a town called Jabiru and it is anticipated that in the next six months to 12 months Jabiru will have a population of 1000. It is a quite extraordinary contradiction to drive through a park which has Aboriginal settlements and so forth and suddenly to come upon this town, which is like a suburban shopping centre in the middle of a city such as Sydney or Melbourne. It is such a total contradiction and it is so out of place in that area. If the uranium mines go ahead, it is intended that the town will have-these are not my words but the words of the people at the Ranger mine-a population of 10,000 people; that is to say, more than 10 times the total Aboriginal population of the area.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.25)-This morning I questioned the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) on a matter of great concern to me and to the people of my electorate. I refer to our tariff laws and a situation of a tariff in reverse. The Phillips tractor company of Merredin is situated in a town approximately halfway between Perth and Kalgoorlie, 260 kilometres from Perth. It is central to the eastern wheat belt of Western Australia and has a population of approximately 4,000 people. It is a relatively large town in Western Australian terms. Laurie Phillips, who has been a resident there for many years, commenced manufacturing four wheel drive agricultural tractors in 1975. His decision to choose the town of Merredin could be justified only by its proximity to the customers. As a present day employer of 50 people, Mr Phillips stands as my ideal of an operater of a successful and decentralised business. He is taking advantage of the position but, more importantly, he is creating work for people who choose not to work in city areas but wish to gain some of the benefits provided by country living. His production over the first five years averaged 13 tractors a year. This was caused more by his lack of working capital than by a lack of orders. He just manufactured to order, factory direct, as he could neither afford stock on hand nor agent's commission.
In 1980 Mr Phillips took a partner and appointed agents both in Western Australia and

1224 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Adjournment

interstate. These moves saw sales rise to 47 tractors in 1981 and to a predicted 70 tractors in 1982. His range of tractors has also increased and now varies from a 249 horsepower, four wheel drive, manual drive machine at a price of about $70,000 to a SIS horsepower, power shift model at $251,000. Laurie has tended to keep ahead of imported tractors both in horsepower and in equipment such as power shift. I can tell honourable members that, from observing his tractors myself, they are advanced models which are at-
tractive to the farming community in this day and age of big equipment. Nevertheless, it is natural with such a small volume producer that Phillips utilise imported mechanical components such as Mercedes engines and Japanese transmissions and axles. The success of his combination is evident from the fact that the first machine sold is still in active service although the purchaser now owns a second machine. That gentleman has resisted all
overtures to sell the original machine back to the Phillips company.
There is a problem. The Federal revenue duty of 2 per cent is applicable to all his imported components. This represents a cost of $600 to $900 a tractor and, in terms of the Phillips production level, about $1,000 a week. Unfortunately for this small and viable four wheel drive tractor manufacturer his competitors, who are importing fully built up four wheel drive tractors from the United States of America in particular, do not pay this duty. In fact they pay no import duties at all. A General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade conference of the past, no doubt recognising that Australia had no manufacturers in this area, saw a duty-free deal in fully built up, four wheel drive tractors as a logical trade-off for concessions overseas in other trade areas. Mr Phillips is quite happy to meet head on and on equal terms the competition from the big time overseas competitors. However, because of this GATT agreement his competitors escape the 2 per cent revenue

duty. In addition, the percentage of imported components denies him access to the tractor bounty.
Only recently the Government announced that it would dispose of the 2 per cent revenue duty for ships manufactured overseas and imported into this country. I am therefore at a loss to know why for 18 months the Government has constantly advised me that there is no way in which a similar arrangement can be made for people in this manufacturing area. It is unbelievable that such a simple solution cannot be provided. In a highly protected nation such as ours we have a situation where some local manufactures are actually being hit by duty which is not applicable to imported components.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)~ Order! It being 10.30 p.m., the House stands adjourned until I0 a.m. tomorrow.

Tenosynovitis and Carpal Tunnel Syndrome
(Question No. 4392)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
(I) In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction, has there been (a) medical examinations of all workers and (b) an ergonomic assessment of the work process, tasks and work environment made by an ergonomist in all areas where repetitious injuries, for example, tenosynovitis and carpal tunnel syndrome, have been reported; if not, why not.
(2) If so, (a) when were assessments made and (b) what recommendations were made.
(3) Have any recommendations been carried out; if not, why not.
Mr CarIton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3). No comprehensive medical examinations beyond those included in the appointment process are conducted routinely. Medical examinations are, however, arranged when officers or employees report symptoms of apparently job related illnesses.
The significance of repetition strain injury as an occupational health problem has only recently been recognised. My Department has reacted promptly in researching the problem and it is expected that a National Health and Medical Research Council (NHMRC) Occupational Health Guide will be issued in the near future. My Department and statutory authorities within my portfolio will make every effort, subject to the availability of resources, to comply with NHMRC recommendations.
My Department is continuing to consider this problem and to seek advice from relevant authorities on suitable working environments for operators where the possible existence of health hazards is suspected. Existing work places have, of course, been designed, fitted out and equipped having regard to recent Government and State safety regulations and relevant standards and guidelines issued by such bodies as the Standards Association of Australia, the NHMRC and the Department of Science and Technology.
In accordance with the Guidelines on Technological Change issued by the Public Service Board, my Department is consulting with staff associations regarding re-equipment programs in some of its major processing areas. The ergonomic aspects of equipment and work processes in the areas concerned are covered in those consultations.

Public Service: Video Display Unit Operators
(Question No. 4414)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction,
(a) what steps have been taken to make operators of visual display units and screen based equipment aware of the need for a sufficient break from operating this equipment, having

Answers to Questions

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1227

regard to the nature of the task and the need for involvement in substantially different activities during the break, as per Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 entitled 'Operation of Visual Display Units and other Screen Based Equipment'.
(b) what percentage of their working day (i) are operators expected to spend on operating duties and (ii) do operators spend on other duties and
(c) how many keystrokes per 7 hour 21 minute day (i) are operators expected to complete and (ii) do operators actually complete.
Mr N. A. Brown-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(a) Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 has been brought to the attention of all supervisors and operators in the Department. Copies of the memorandum have also been provided to the statutory authorities for which the Attorney General has responsibility; although it should be noted that some of those authorities have autonomous powers in relation to the matters in question. Operators in the Department, in consultation with supervisors, take regular breaks from operating this equipment to perform other tasks or to take basic rest periods.
(b) The percentage of the working day spent on operating duties depends on the nature of the task being performed and it is therefore difficult to give a precise answer. However, experience in the Department indicates that no operators spend more than 75% of their day on operating duties.
(c) (i) The general practice in the Department is for the output required of an operator to be determined by the nature and complexity of the task being performed and there is no requirement to complete a number of keystrokes within any specific time frame. Operators are expected to complete a reasonable number of keystrokes according to the task being undertaken. (ii) No statistics are maintained by the Department on keystrokes completed.
Public Service: Video Display Unit Operators
(Question No. 4418)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction, (a) what steps have been taken to make operators of visual display units and screen based equipment aware of the need for a sufficient break from operating this equipment, having regard to the nature of the task and the need for involvement in substantially different activities during the break, as per Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 entitled 'Operation of Visual Display Units and other Screen Based Equipment', (b) what percentage of their working day (i) are operators expected to spend on operating duties and (ii) do operators spend on other duties and (c) how many keystrokes per 7 hours 21 minute day (i) are operators expected to complete and (ii) do operators actually complete.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) operators have been made aware of the need for breaks by supervisors and/or by circulars and are permitted regular breaks from operating their equipment.
(b) The percentage of the working day occupied on operating duties varies considerably depending on the system utilised and work organisation. Where operators spend lOOper cent of their working time on operating duties they are allowed regular breaks additional to lunch and morning and afternoon

tea. In systems where operators spend a substantial proportion of their time on other duties the work arrangements themselves provide time away from equipment operating duties.
(c) The type of equipment and the processing system used influence the rates of output expected from operators. Operators who use screen based equipment for processing medical benefits and similar claims are expected to complete, on average, 48,750 keystrokes per 6t hour day (7,500 per hour). The 6t hour day is based on a 7 hour 21 minute day less morning and afternoon tea breaks and rest breaks. Actual keystrokes rates vary between approximately 7,500 per hour and 12,000 per hour. In a number of operational systems there is no requirement to complete a set number of keystrokes per day because of the nature of the work involved, for example, checking the information shown on a visual display unit.

Report on the Health Needs of Adolescents
(Question No. 4485)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 27 April 1982:
When will the Minister announce the Government's support or rejection of the recommendations in the report on the health needs of adolescents.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The implementation of most of the recommendations in this discussion document falls within the responsibilities of the State and Territory Governments and health authorities and voluntary agencies.
The Capital Territory Health Commission agrees in principle with the recommendations of the report and, subject to constraints associated with current staffing and financial restrictions, supports the progressive implementation of recommendations relevant to the Australian Capital Territory. The likely impact on existing health services in implementing those recommendations which are applicable to the Commission are being examined.
In relation to confidentiality in claiming health benefits, protection of confidentiality for dependants in claiming medical insurance benefits has been discussed previously at a meeting of the Health Insurance Advisory Committee. Health insurance funds appreciate the position of children and student dependants, and generally do what they can to protect their privacy. In relation to dependants of Pensioner Health Benefit and Health Care card-holders, the Department of Social Security will make available duplicate cards on application, subject to adequate proof of identity. These duplicate cards enable the confidentiality of such dependants to be maintained.

Education
(Question No. 4508)
Mr Milton asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 19 April 1982:
(I) What are the cost estimates used for planning and funding purposes by the Tertiary Education Commission required for each equivalent full-time student (EFTS) in colleges of advanced education in Australia in (a) teacher education, (b) science based courses and (c) commerce/social science/humanities based courses in each State and Territory in 1982, 1983 and 1984.
(2) What are the average funded costs for each EFTS in the colleges of advanced education in (a) teacher education, (b) science based courses and (c) commerce/social science/humanities based courses for (i) Australia, (ii) each State and Territory and (iii) each individual college of advanced education for 1982, 1983 and 1984.
Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The factors which were taken into account by the advanced education Council in preparing its Advice to the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission (CTEC) in respect of recommendations for funding in the 1982-84 triennium are set out at paragraph 8.27 et seq of Volume I, Part 3 of the Commission's 'Report for 1982-84 Triennium '. The average cost estimate used by the Advanced Education Council was approximately $4,000 per equivalent full-time student (EFTS), which represented a 5.5 per cent increase on the existing level of support. The figure is expressed in estimated December 1980 costs levels. The CTEC did not support in full the Advanced Education Council's funding proposals (see paragraph 8.52 to 8.57 of Volume I Part I of the Commission's Report). It recommended a level of general recurrent funding some 5 per cent below that sought by the Council.
(2) In accordance with long standing arrangements, recurrent grants to higher education institutions are allocated by the Commonwealth on the basis of a block grant. The expenditure of that grant, including the allocation of funds to particular disciplines or areas of teaching, is a matter for the institution to determine. Consistent with this policy, the CTEC does not publish cost parameters used in developing its recurrent grant recommendations.
In the case of colleges of advanced education, these recommendations are based on advice from State authorities, in accordance with an agreement between the Commonwealth and State Ministers for Education made in June 1979. Under this arrangement, the allocation of funds for recurrent purposes in the advanced education sector by the CTEC is based on the folowing procedures:
(a) The total number of advanced education students and their mix in terms of individual institutions and fields and levels of study in a given State will be determined by the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission on the advice of the Advanced Education Council after consultation with State authorities.
(b) The Commission, on the advice of the Advanced Education Council, will allocate the available funds to individual colleges after advice to the Council from the appropriate State authorities. As the development of individual colleges is largely a matter for the States in the light of co-ordinated

Vietnam
(Question No. 4567)

Australian Citizenship
(Question No. 4587)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Did the Prime Minister in his policy statement on 30 September 1980, promise that the Government will encourage migrants to obtain Australian citizenship.
(2) If so, when was this promise implemented and in what form.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The policy statement provided for a public awareness program to encourage migrants to obtain citizenship.
(2) Implementation is on-going. The Government has taken various initiatives to enhance the status, and increase awareness, of Australian citizenship. These initiatives include amendment of the Commonwealth Electoral Act to make Australian citizenship the basis for the franchise and for nomination for Parliament; a Task Force of the Australian Council on Population and Ethnic Affairs has examined citizenship enhancement strategies; a study of the factors which lead people to take out citizenship has been commissioned and is now completed; a review of the initiatives and measures in other countries, especially the ways and means of greater involvement of the community in the acquisition of citizenship, is underway. There has also been consideration of how Australian non-government organisations can participate in and contribute to the citizenship process and consultations are continuing. A review of the Australian Citizenship Act has been finalised and proposals for changes to the Act are currently available for public discussion. Community consultations on the proposed changes to the Citizenship Act will serve to further focus attention on, and increase awareness of, all aspects of citizenship.
Isolated Students in Remote Areas
(Question No. 4595)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Did the Prime Minister in his policy statement on 30 September 1980 promise a 3 year pilot program to provide onloan video facilities for isolated students in remote areas, beginning with students of the School of the Air.
(2) If so, when was this promise implemented and in what form.
(3) What was the Commonwealth's appropriation and spending on this program in (a) 1980-81 and (b) 1981-82.
Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The announcement of a 3 year pilot program to provide on-loan video facilities for isolated students in remote areas was made by the Prime Minister in his policy statement on 30 September 1980.
(2) After consultations between the Commonwealth and State Governments during 1981, agreement was reached on the allocation of funds to each State under the Loan Video Program. The program commenced from the beginning of 1982.
Each State will receive an agreed share of the $4m available to cover the three calendar years of the program's operation,

namely 1982 to 1984. Allocations to States have been calculated on the basis of the estimated numbers of isolated children studying at primary level by correspondence. The following table sets out the States' shares:

In accordance with agreements reached, each State is free to make its own decisions on how best to use the allocated funds, provided they are directed at improving the access of isolated primary students to educational video or other audiovisual facilities. In most States this involves the purchase and loan to isolated families of video recorders and television monitors, as well as the production and distribution of video taped educational material.

States will be required after the end of each financial year to provide the Commonwealth with a detailed statement of how the funds have been spent.

(3) (a) Consultations were held with the States during 1980-81 but there was no expenditure of program funds.

(b) Appropriation and expected expenditure $1.34m.

National Language Policy
(Question No. 4635)
Dr Theophanous asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
Has the Government considered any action towards the development of a national language policy covering areas such as community languages, English as a second language and ethnic schools in accordance with the resolutions of the Ethnic Communities Councils of New South Wales and Victoria.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The general issue of the development of a national language policy is one that has been referred to a Committee in another place for investigation. The resolutions of the Victorian and New South Wales Ethnic Communities Councils Conferences on a national language policy, together with those from other State and Territory conferences, will form an important input to a national conference sponsored by the Federation of Ethnic Communities Councils in Canberra in October 1982. I hope that the recommendations of that Conference will form the basis of a substantial submission to the Committee's inquiry.
The issues involved in developing a national language policy touch on many aspects of Australian life. They include considerations relating to English language teaching, community languages, the role of languages in a multicultural society as well as our language needs for trade, diplomacy, cultural exchange, defence and tourism purposes. I therefore urge every interested person and organisation to ensure that they make their views known to the Committee when submissions are called for in the coming months.

Translators and Interpreters
(Question No. 4637)
Dr Theophanous asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:

1230 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Answers to Questions

(I) Can he indicate when the review of the National Accreditation Authority for Translators and Interpreters will be completed.
(2) Will the review take into account the need for new educational programs for the training of translators.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) When the National Accreditation Authority for Translators and Interpreters (NAAT1) was established in 1977, it was envisaged that responsibility for the profession would, within five years, be assumed by a successor~body or bodies. Planning is under way for the devolution, at the beginning of 1983, of NAATl's functions to a national registration body and a national professional association.
The national registration body will have responsibility for the maintenance of standards~corresponding with the five levels of accreditation established by NAATI~for testing and other forms of assessment, for accreditation and for registration. The registration body, which will be incorporated as a company, will aim to become self-funding as rapidly as possible, but in its early years of operation will require government subsidies.
It is envisaged that the main functions of the national professional association will be to protect and further the interests of the profession, and represent its views, to maintain

discipline within the profession and uphold professional ethics, and to maintain liaison with the national registration body on all matters affecting the profession. No governmental action will be required towards the establishment of the association, but NAATI will do all that it can to encourage the emergence of an association, possibly based on a federation of existing association.
(2) NAATI has encouraged the establishment of courses for interpreters and translators by maintaining contact with educational institutions, with the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission and, where appropriate, with State and Territory post-secondary education authorities to advise on the need for training courses. The Authority is not in a position to fund educational programs, apart from occasional intensive training workshops designed to prepare candidates for NAATI tests.
Panels of experts composed principally of NAATI members and, where necessary, consultants with expertise in the languagejs concerned examine each course in person advising on the course content needed for NAATI approval and, where those standards are met, recommending the course to the Authority for approval.
Four training courses for interpreters and translators were being run by institutions at the time NAATI was established. A list of the eighteen courses now being conducted or which institutions plan to introduce in 1983 is given below.

All Hallow's School, Five Dock
(Question No. 4650)
Mr Maher asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
In view of the Minister's inspection of All Hallow's School, Halley Street, Five Dock, New South Wales, will the school be eligible to obtain a grant for capital building works,
Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The Commonwealth Schools Commission's Planning and Finance Committee in New South Wales, the body responsible for making recommendations to the Commission on the capital needs of non-governemnt schools received an application for assistance from All Hallow's School on 16 June 1982. The school subsequently, on 20 July 1982, submitted a revised application which sought assistance with the demolition of the existing school and the construction of a new school.
Members of the Committee, which considers each application on the basis of the relative educational and financial need, visited the school on 29 July 1982 to discuss the application. A building consultant has also visited the school to discuss the various planning options with the school authorities.
It is anticipated that the school's application will be considered at a full Committee meeting in late August 1982. It will be appreciated that, in order to assess the relative claims of all applicant schools, the Committee must undertake a detailed examination of each school's application to assess the relative educational and financial need of each school. All Hallow's can be assured that the process of making a decision in relation to its application will be undertaken as expeditiously as possible. As soon as a decision has been made, the school will be advised accordingly.
The honourable member should be aware that demand from non-government schools for Commonwealth capital assistance far exceeds the funds available. The Commonwealth Schools Commission recently reported that in 1981 the New South Wales Planning and Finance Committee received 143 applications from non-government schools for capital grants. The applications involved grants which amounted to $53.1 m. Seventy-two of the applications, that is 50.3 per cent, were rejected due to lack of funds, even though 10 of them, involving grants worth about $4.6m. were given an A rating (top priority) in terms of both educational and financial need.

Unemployment
(Question No. 4671)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to Labour Forces Survey Data in January 1982 which shows that unemployed workers in the 60-64 year age bracket are expected to remain unemployed for an average of 2 years.
(2) Would those same people, in January 1981, only have remained unemployed for an average of 43 weeks.
(3) Do many older unemployed workers drop out of the job market when they discoverthe limited job opportunities available and the age discrimination by employers.
(4) What steps has his Department taken or will be taking to tackle the problems of age discrimination by employers and the psychological trauma suffered by older workers who are forced out of the work force and must remain unemployed for longer periods of time.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) I am informed by the Australian Statistician that there are no official statistics which show that in January 1982 unemployed workers in the 60-64 year age bracket were expected to remain unemployed for an average of two years.
The monthly labour force survey conducted by the Australian Bureau of Statistics produces estimates of the actual duration of unemployment up to the time of the survey-not the expected duration-for the following four age groups: 15-19; 20-24; 25-34; 35-54.
Information is compiled by the ABS for certain other age groups but, because the number of people in these categories who are selected in the sample is very small, the figures are not reliable because of the associated large sampling errors.
(3) The most recent statistics available on persons, classified by the ABS as 'discouraged job seekers' (that is, persons who wanted a job but were not actively looking for work because, for a variety of reasons, they believed they would not be able to find a job) were collected in September 1981. It is estimated that in September 1981 there were about 9, I00 discouraged job seekers 55 to 64 years of age who thought they would be considered by prospective employers to be too old to work. Source: ABS Supplementary Labour Force Survey, September 1981 (unpublished data).

1232 REPRESENTATIVES 8 September 1982

Answers to Questions

(4) My Department is currently undertaking an examination of the position of older unemployed job seekers registered with the Commonwealth Employment Service so that appropriate assistance can be given. In addition, my Department's Bureau of Labour Market Research is planning a major study of unemployment among older workers which is due to be completed by June next year. This information will further increase my Department's capacity to develop soundly-based measures of assistance to counter employment disadvantages suffered by older workers.
Citizenship
(Question No. 4676)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Why have delays been experienced for some time between the time of application for citizenship and citizenship actually being granted.
(2) Can we give reasons for these delays and indicate the average waiting periods currently experienced by applicants.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The numbers of applications for citizenship have been increasing for some time and are currently 38 per cent above application levels last year. This higher application rate has resulted in the delays experienced in the processing of applications for citizenship.
The current average national processing time between date of receipt of an application for Australian citizenship until date of decision (approval or refusal) is 22 weeks. On average, a further 6 weeks elapses for conferral of citizenship at a departmental ceremony. Public ceremonies are conducted by local government authorities and the waiting period for these ceremonies varies considerably according to the size of the municipality and number of conferrals undertaken by the particular authority.
Action is currently being taken to reduce bottlenecks and streamline processing procedures generally.
Office of Technology Assessment
(Question No. 4696)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on Tuesday 17 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the creation, in 1972, of the Office of Technology Assessment to serve the United States Congress, with the primary function of providing Congress with assessments that identify the range of consequences of policy alternatives affecting technology.
(2) If so, is he able to say whether the Board of the Office consists of 6 senators and 6 members of the House of Representatives assisted by a staff of consultants.
(3) Has the Australian Government given consideration to establishing such an office.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Office of Technology Assessment (OTA) is governed by a Congressional Board consisting of six senators and six members of the House of Representatives. An Advisory Council of ten public members eminent in science, technology and education advises the Board on OTA assessments and other matters. The OTA has an in-house staff of 80-90 professionalofficers.

(3) On 18 April 1980 the Committee of Inquiry into Technological Change in Australia reported to the Prime Minister. The Committee recommended:
that a Standing Committee of ASTEC be established with representatives from industry, the trade unions, scientists and technologists and government (including State Governments on a rotating basis), and with terms of reference as follows:
to review on a continuing basis the processes and trends in technological change in Australia and elsewhere; and to evaluate and report on the direct and indirect effects of technological change at the national level (Recommendation 28) that a Council of Technological Change be established comprising Commonwealth and State Ministers (Recommendation 29).
In the Government's response, Recommendation 28 was accepted and the Technological Change Committee is now in operation as a standing. committee of ASTEC. Recommendation 29 was not accepted, rather it was decided to draw the attention of existing Ministerial bodies, such as the Commonwealth-State Labour and Industry Ministers' Conferences, to the need for adequate consultation and cooperation in matters relating to technological change.
Medical Benefits
(Question No. 4718)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) When did he decide on changes to the Medical Benefits Schedule which were outlined in the Medical Benefits Circular No. 266.
(2) On what date and by what means did he convey this information to (a) registered funds and (b) relevant medical organisations.
(3) Who were the groups referred to in part 2 (b).
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Approval in principle to the amendments to the Medical Benefits Schedule which were outlined in Medical Benefits Circular No. 266 was given by the then Minister for Health, on 29 March 1982. I formally approved the amendments on 19 May 1982.
(2)-(3) Information relating to these amendments was conveyed initially by M. B. Circular No. 266 distributed on 12 May 1982. This was followed by a revised edition of the Medical Benefits Schedule Book incorporating all the amendments. Distribution of the book commenced on 9 June 1982.
M. B. Circular No. 266 was distributed to the Australian Medical Association and to all registered health insurance organizations and the Medical Benefits Schedule Book to all practising medical practitioners and all registered health insurance organisations.

Electric-powered Motor Vehicles: Sales Tax Exemption
(Question No. 4755)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Has he received a submission from the National Secretariat of the Australian Electric Vehicle Association seeking exemption from sales tax for components used in the after market conversion of motor vehicles to electric drive; if so, has the Government given consideration to the possible saving on fuel bills to this country by the introduction of more electric vehicles.
(2) Will the Government, in the interests of the future conservation of power resources in Australia, give aid to this industry to provide a viable alternative to the petrol driven vehicle.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The submission referred to has been received by the Treasurer and has been considered.
(2) It is appreciated that electric-powered motor vehicles can have a number of advantages over petrol-powered alternatives. Most of these advantages would, however, benefit directly the purchaser of the vehicle who can be expected to weigh such benefits against the relative purchase prices of the two types of vehicle. It is relevant here that the Government's policy of import parity pricing of crude oil is itself broad encouragement to shift from petrol-powered vehicles to those using alternative sources of energy. In the circumstances, there appears to be no need for additional incentives to encourage the use of electric power in the transport area.
Independent Audit of Funds Granted to Non-government Organisations
(Question No. 4786)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:

What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The requirements of my Department are prescribed in Finance Directions Section 13/13 issued by the Secretary, Department of Finance, pursuant to Finance Regulation 127A. Essentially the Department requires organisations to vouch the proper expenditure of funds provided either in their normal audited financial statements in the case of general assistance grants or, in the case of specific purpose grants, by providing separate statements prepared by a qualified public accountant who is not a member of the organisation.
In the case of grants made under the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Act 1976, technical and cost investigation staff of the Board established under that Act perform technical and financial audits in relation to funds provided and the purposes for which they were provided.

Independent Audit of Funds Granted to Non-government Organisations
(Question No. 4792)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
To ensure proper accountability for the expenditure of Commonwealth funds my Department has prescribed rules relating to grants made by the Department to nongovernment organisations and one of the requirements is that each organisation should appoint an independent auditor to audit the organisation's annual financial statements in which each grant must be identified and accounted for separately.

Concord Repatriation Hospital
(Question No, 4919)
Mr Maher asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
(I) What number of landings by helicopters have been made within the grounds of Concord Repatriation Hospital since I July 1981.
(2) What equipment is held at the Hospital to cope with a helicopter crashing (a) on land and (b) in the adjoining Parramatta River.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Since I July 1981 there have been seven landings by helicopters in the grounds of Repatriation General Hospital, Concord. These landings have all been associated with medical emergencies. The helipad at the Repatriation General Hospital is not used for routine landings.
(2) (a) The following equipment is available at the Repatriation General Hospital, Concord, for rescues on land:
(I) fire tender, including an additional capacity of approximately two hundred litres of foam;
(2) electrical fire extinguishers; (3) fuel fire extinguishers; (4) magnesium extinguishers (dry chemical for use in both
electrical and fuel fires); (5) a 'group call' paging system for alerting the disaster
team; (6) general stores for use in disaster situations.
(b) Special equipment is not available at the Hospital for rescues from water. Community emergency services would be utilised and supplemented by the full range of emergency measures available from within the Hospital.
Answers to Questions on Notice
(Question No. 4953)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
(I) What are the details of the Minister's arrangements for the answering of questions on notice, specifying what officers (a) on the Minister's personal staff and (b) within the Minister's Department, are responsible for (i) examining the question asked and (ii) preparing the answers.

Answers to Questions

8 September 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1237

(2) How many questions on notice has the Minister been asked from 25 November 1980 to date.
(3) What is the average delay incurred between the time of questions being placed on notice and the provision of the answer.
(4) For each question referred to in part (2), how many have taken (a) 1-3 months, (b) 3-6 months, (c) 6-9 months, (d) 9-12 months and (e) over 12 months, to be answered.
(5) How many of these questions are currently still on notice, and what is the reason for delay in the case of each question which has been on the Notice Paper for 12 months or more.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The distribution and co-ordination of questions on notice is the responsibility of the Parliamentary Questions Officer within the Department who allocates each question to that area of the Central Office of the Department with responsibility for the subject matter. A response is prepared by an officer in the relevant area and approved by the Divisional Head. Following the Secretary's endorsement of the proposed answer it is sent to the Minister's Senior Private Secretary for the Minister's approval.
(2) 193.
(3) 62 days.
(4) (a) 86; (b) 36; (c) 4; (d) none; (e) none.
(5) 23, none of which has been on the Notice Paper for 12 months or more.
Water Research
(Question No. 4977)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 26 August 1982:
(I) Did the report of the Australian Water Resource Council (A WRC) Working Group on water research policy state that examination of other research programs indicates that overall success relies heavily on the calibre of the full-time support staff and in this regard criticism has been directed at the support provided by the Minister's Department for the AWRC water research program.
(2) Has the Government accepted the recommendation of the Working Group that a National Office of Water Research be established within the Minister's Department with a full time director and adequate support staff to service the new National Water Research Council; if not, why not.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes
(2) The Government has decided not to establish a National Office of Water Research at this time, and the support staff requirements for the National Water Research Council will be taken up by my Department shortly with the Public Service Board.
River Murray
(Question No, 4978)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 26 August 1982:

(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to an article in the Adelaide News of 28 July 1982, entitled 'Mighty Murray now a dam' and, in particular, the comments of the South Australian Minister for Water Resources that (a) the State Government would seek to transfer responsibility for the river mouth to the River Murray Commission and (b) Victoria, New South Wales and the Commonwealth should at least share the cost of keeping the river mouth dredged.
(2) Has the Government been approached by the South Australian Government in relation to these matters, and does the Minister support the State Government's proposals.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.
(2) The Government has not been approached by South Australia on these matters, but the River Murray Commission would be the appropriate forum for initial discussions.
Nugan Hand Group
Mr Newman-On 6 May 1982 (Hansard, page 2334) Mr Hayden asked me a question without notice concerning two reports by a person who was formerly an Army Investigator. He asked whether I was aware of the existence of a second report which, he indicated, dealt with illegal activities by Nugan Hand, their associates and other persons who are prominent in public life,
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I advised on 6 May that I was aware of the existence of one report and undertook to check further to see whether there was a second report.
A copy of the second report has since been located. It is dated 17 February 1971 and was a follow-up report to the first report dated 19 January 1971. The first report had dealt primarily with some Army personnel involvement with black marketing; the second report had dealt with civilian personnel that came to notice during the black marketing investigation.
I am advised that careful examination of the report has failed to reveal any such mention or evidence of any connections with Nugan Hand, their associates or other persons prominent in public life. This view has been reached after enquiry into the appropriate Department of Defence records and enquiries to the Commonwealth/New South Wales Joint Task Force into Drug Trafficking. Furthermore, I am advised that an outline of the two reports appeared in the Melbourne Sun Herald of 13 March 1971.
In relation to the requested information on action taken by the authorities following receipt of those reports, I have been advised by my colleague, the Minister for Defence, that the first report resulted in subsequent disciplinary charges against certain members of the Australian Army. In respect of the second report, my colleague advised that the report had been requested by an officer of the then Department of External Affairs on 23 January 1971 and had been forwarded to the Secretary of that Department on 3 March 1971, for any further enquiry by that Department in relation to the Australian Embassy in South Vietnam.
